Proof of God's Dissimilarity to Creation
Sanusi states the meaning of divine dissimilarity and gives its rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God is unlike any created thing and why similarity would entail impossibility.

Core Idea
If God were like creation, He would be created—and that is impossible.

This lesson unpacks the doctrine of divine dissimilarity (mukhālafa lil-ḥawādith). The author first defines what it means—God is not characterized by any attributes of creation—then provides a concise logical proof: similarity would necessitate originatedness and need, which cannot apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: definition and proof.
The passage is dense but short.
2
Identify the definition and the proof
Find where the author defines 'dissimilarity' by negating specific created attributes. Then locate the conditional argument: if similarity then origination and need.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Ask yourself why the author calls this a 'proof' and how it strengthens the belief in tawḥīd.
This connects to the broader Islamic creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفة للحوادث بالاتحاد، ولا بالاتصال ولا بالانفصال ولا باليمين ولا بالشّمال، ولا بالخلف ولا بالأمام، ولا بغير ذلك من صفات الحوادث. دليل مخالفته تعالى للحوادث إذ لو كان مماثلاً لها، لوجب له تعالى ما وجب لها من الحدوث والافتقار ، وذلك محال لما مرّ . وفي الحديث : «القلبُ بيتُ الرَّبِّ» ، وتأويله أن تقول قوله : وإنَّما وسعني ؛ أي: وسع هيبتي ورحمتي. وقوله : «القلب بيت الرَّبِّ أي : محلُّ رحمته وتجليه، وذلك لأنَّ النَّوع الإنساني مهبط أوامر الله ونواهيه، إذ هو المتحمّل للأمانة التي عرضت على السَّماوات والأرض فأبين أن يحملنها وأشفقن منها . قوله: (ولا بالاتصال... إلخ) : أي : ما ورد مما يوهم الاتصال مؤوّل، ففي الحديث القدسي : ولا يزال عبدي يتقرَّبُ إليَّ بالنوافل حتَّى أحبَّه، فإذا أحببته؛ كنتُ سمعه الذي يسمع به، وبصره الذي يُبصر به، ورِجْلَه التي يمشي بها ، ويده التي يبطش بها ، وتأويله : أنَّ ذلك كناية عن استيلاء محبَّة الله على الشَّخص حتّى أغنته عن شهود سواه. قوله : (إذ لو كان مماثلاً لها ... إلخ): شروع في الدليل على المخالفة . واعلم : أنّ العالم وإن عَظُم في نفسه فهو بالنسبة لعظم قدرته تعالى ليس بشيء، فكيف يكون العليُّ الكبير، القديم القدير، حالاً أو متّصلاً أو منفصلاً أو مستَقِرّاً أو على جهة لهذا الشيء الحقير الحادث الفقير .
Dissimilarity to created things: not by union, nor by connection, nor by disconnection, nor by right, nor by left, nor by back, nor by front, nor by anything else of the attributes of created things. The proof of His exaltedness's dissimilarity to created things: For if He were similar to them, then what is necessary for them—origination and neediness—would be necessary for Him, and that is impossible for what has passed. And in the hadith: 'The heart is the house of the Lord,' and its interpretation is that you say: 'He said: 'and it did not contain Me' means: it contained My awe and My mercy.' And His saying: 'The heart is the house of the Lord' means: the locus of His mercy and His manifestation, and that is because the human species is the descent of God's commands and prohibitions, since it is the bearer of the trust that was offered to the heavens and the earth and they refused to carry it and feared from it. His saying: '(and not by connection... etc.)' means: what has come that implies connection is interpretable. In the sacred hadith: 'My servant does not cease drawing near to Me with supererogatory acts until I love him, and when I love him, I am his hearing with which he hears, his sight with which he sees, his leg with which he walks, and his hand with which he strikes,' and its interpretation is that it is a metaphor for the domination of God's love over the person until it makes him independent of witnessing other than Him. His saying: '(For if He were similar to them... etc.)' is the beginning of the proof of dissimilarity. And know: that the world, even if it is great in itself, is in relation to the greatness of His power not a thing, so how can the Most High, the Great, the Eternal, the All-Powerful be a state, or connected, or separated, or settled, or in a direction for this lowly, originated, needy thing?
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the negative attribute of divine dissimilarity (mukhālafa lil-ḥawādith). It begins by specifying types of created attributes that God is not described by: union, connection, disconnection, right/left, back/front, and any similar attributes. Then it provides a proof: if God resembled created things, He would share their properties of origination (ḥudūth) and neediness (iftiqār), which is impossible given what was established earlier about His necessary existence. The author then addresses two hadiths that might suggest God is in a place or has body parts. The first hadith 'the heart is the house of the Lord' is interpreted as referring to God's awe and mercy, and the heart being the locus of divine commands. The second hadith about God becoming the hearing, sight, etc., of the servant is interpreted as a metaphor for the overwhelming love of God that makes the servant oblivious to creation. Finally, a concluding remark emphasizes that the entire world is nothing before God's power, so how could He be in any way associated with lowly created things?

Key Terms
مخالفة للحوادث
مماثلة
حدوث
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'dissimilar' means God is completely different from creation in every way, but the correct understanding is that He is not contingent like creation.
Correction: The proof shows that dissimilarity means the absence of contingent properties, not merely a difference in degree.

Checkpoint
What is the core proof given for God's dissimilarity to created things?
A) God is unlike creation because He is eternal.
B) If God were similar to creation, He would have origination and neediness, which is impossible.
C) The hadiths clearly state God is unlike creation.
D) The world is too small for God.

Key Takeaway
The core proof for tanzih: if God were like creation, He would be created—impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you ever catch yourself picturing Him with human-like features or in a specific direction? How can you train your heart to worship a God who is absolutely unlike anything you've ever seen?
?
If God were like us, He would need someone to bring Him into existence. Do you ever act as if God needs your worship or obedience? What would it mean to truly believe He is self-sufficient?
?
The proof says similarity to creation would make God originated. Does your daily life reflect that God is the Creator, not part of His creation? In what specific ways can you live that truth more deeply starting today?

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Hadiths: The Heart and the Nearness
Sanusi interprets two ambiguous hadiths to show they do not imply anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author interprets two problematic hadiths that might imply God is in a place or has body parts.

Core Idea
Apparent anthropomorphic expressions in hadith are metaphors for divine mercy and love.

The author addresses two hadiths that could be misunderstood as describing God in human terms. The first depicts the heart as God's 'house'; the second describes God becoming the servant's limbs. He provides sound interpretations that preserve divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read both hadiths and their interpretations
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the two hadiths and how the author explains each one.
The first hadith is about the heart being God's house; the second is about God becoming the servant's hearing, etc.
2
Identify the hermeneutical principle
Notice that the author uses ta'wil to avoid literal meanings that would imply God has a place or body parts.
Ta'wil is a standard method in Sunni theology.
3
Understand why ta'wil is needed
Reflect on why these hadiths cannot be taken literally: they would contradict the established proof of God's dissimilarity.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even the heart's closeness to God is a metaphor for mercy, not a physical indwelling. Is your relationship with God rooted in awe of His transcendence, or do you sometimes imagine Him as too familiar or anthropomorphic? Take a moment to repent of any imagined likeness and reaffirm your belief in tanzih.
?
The hadith about God becoming the servant's hearing and sight is a metaphor for love that overcomes all distractions. In your daily worship, does remembrance of God ever reach a point where you forget yourself? What worldly attachments still hold you back from that state, and what step can you take today to lessen their grip?

The Three Aspects of Divine Unity
Al-Sawi enumerates the three aspects of tawhid and explains their negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of divine unity (tawhid) as they pertain to God's essence, attributes, and actions, and how each aspect negates any form of multiplicity or similarity to creation.

Core Idea
Divine unity has three dimensions: essence, attributes, and actions – each negating multiplicity.

Al-Sawi, in his commentary, explains the foundational creedal principle of tawhid by breaking it down into three distinct aspects: oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of actions. This passage clarifies what each aspect means and what it negates, establishing that God is absolutely unique in His being, His qualities, and His agency. Understanding these three divisions is essential for a correct Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tawhid
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the passage is structured around three key phrases marked by 'أو' and 'و'.
2
Identify the three aspects
Look for the three categories: oneness of essence (وحدانية الذات), oneness of attributes (وحدانية الصفات), and oneness of actions (وحدانية الأفعال). Note what each negates.
The text uses 'أو' to introduce the second and 'و' for the third.
3
Understand the negations
For each aspect, identify what is being negated: for essence, negation of composition and parallel; for attributes, negation of continuous and discrete quantity; for actions, negation of any other agent.
The commentary explains the proof for each.
4
Reflect on the overall unity
Consider how these three aspects together comprise the complete tawhid. Why is it necessary to affirm all three?
This connects to avoiding shirk in all its forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوحدانية
ووجود ذات أخرى تماثل الذات العليَّة؛ أي: أنَّه تعالى ليست ذاتُه مركَّبة من أجزاء متّصل بعضُها ببعض، وإلا لكان مماثلاً للحوادث من حيث التركيب، فيحتاج إلى من يُركبه، وهو محال .
وليس له نظير في ذاته .
(أو) أي : وعدم الإثنينية في (صفاته العليَّة) اتصالاً أو انفصالاً أيضاً، فوحدانية الصفات تنفي عنه تعالى الكمّ المتصل والمنفصل فيها ؛ أي : تنفي العدد في حقيقة كلِّ واحدة منها ، متّصلاً كان أو منفصلاً ؛ أي : أنَّه تعالى له حياة واحدة، وعِلْمٌ واحد، وهكذا لا أكثر.
وليس ثَمَّ من يتّصف بصفات الألوهية سواه تعالى.
(و) وحدانية؛ أي عدم الإثنينيَّة في (الفعل) يعني : متّصف بوحدانية الأفعال، فليس ثُمَّ من له فعل من الأفعال سواه تعالى، إذ كل ما سواه عاجز، لا تأثير له في شيء من الأشياء.
وقوله : ( ووجود ذات أخرى : راجع للمنفصل، فهو لفّ ونشر مرتب.
Unity. And the existence of another essence similar to the supreme essence means that His essence, may He be exalted, is not composed of parts connected to each other, otherwise it would be similar to contingent beings in terms of composition, and would need a composer, which is impossible. And He has no parallel in His essence. (Or) meaning: and the negation of duality in His lofty attributes, whether connected or separate as well. So the unity of attributes negates from Him, exalted, continuous and discrete quantity in them; i.e., it negates number in the reality of each one of them, whether continuous or discrete; i.e., He, exalted, has one life, one knowledge, and so on, no more. And there is no one besides Him who is described with the attributes of divinity. (And) unity, i.e., negation of duality in action, meaning He is described with unity of actions, so there is no one who has any action from among the actions besides Him, exalted, since everything other than Him is incapable, having no influence in anything. And His statement: 'and the existence of another essence' refers to the discrete, so it is an ordered dispersal and wrapping.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the three aspects of divine unity (tawhid) as they pertain to essence, attributes, and actions. The author begins with oneness of essence (وحدانية الذات), explaining that it negates any composition in God's essence. If His essence were composed of parts, it would resemble contingent beings and would require a composer, which is impossible. Then he states that there is no parallel to His essence.

Next, oneness of attributes (وحدانية الصفات) is discussed. This negates both continuous and discrete quantity in the attributes. In each attribute (e.g., life, knowledge) there is only one, not multiple. There is no other being that possesses divine attributes.

Finally, oneness of actions (وحدانية الأفعال) negates any other agent beside God. Everything other than God is incapable and has no real effect.

The commentary also clarifies that the phrase 'existence of another essence' refers to the discrete aspect, and that the text uses a literary device (لف ونشر مرتب) where items are mentioned in one order and explained in a matching order.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال
الإثنينية
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's attributes imply multiple eternal beings or parts in God.
Correction: The passage teaches that each attribute is one and negates both continuous and discrete quantity, so attributes are not multiple in a numeral sense that would imply compositeness.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'oneness of attributes' (وحدانية الصفات) negate?
A) That God has any attributes at all.
B) Both continuous and discrete quantity in each attribute.
C) Only discrete quantity in attributes.
D) That attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The complete tawhid involves affirming that God is one in essence, attributes, and actions, with absolutely no multiplicity or partner in any of these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely believe that every single thing that happens in your life—from the smallest success to the greatest trial—comes from Allah alone, without any real contribution from causes? How does this belief shape your reactions when things go wrong?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as multiple, separate things? What would change in your worship if you truly felt each attribute is a single, perfect reality without any hint of multiplicity?
?
Are there moments when you attribute outcomes to your own efforts, intelligence, or the help of others, forgetting that all actions are entirely from Allah? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself of the oneness of actions in your daily life?

The Logical Proof of Mutual Hindrance
Al-Sawi presents the core logical argument for the impossibility of multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of burhān al-tamānuʿ and why it proves the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Multiple gods would logically entail mutual hindrance, which is impossible.

This lesson presents the central logical argument for tawhid known as burhān al-tamānuʿ. Al-Sawi shows that if there were multiple gods, they could potentially conflict, leading to absurd consequences. The proof is concise but rigorous, relying on the impossibility of combining opposites or attributing impotence to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'if multiplicity were possible'.
The proof is a conditional chain; understanding each link is crucial.
2
Identify the key implication
Al-Sawi argues that multiplicity implies the possibility of mutual hindrance. Understand why: if two gods exist, each can will opposite things, and that possibility is inherent.
The proof does not require actual conflict, only the logical possibility.
3
Analyze the two outcomes
When two gods will opposite actions, either both wills are realized (impossible because opposites cannot coexist) or neither is realized (implying impotence). Trace this choice.
Impotence is a sign of contingency (ḥudūth) and need, incompatible with divinity.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since the possibility of mutual hindrance leads to impossibility, and multiplicity necessitates this possibility, multiplicity itself must be impossible.
This establishes tawhid as a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أنَّه لو أمكن التَّعدُّد) : أي : في الذات والصفات والأفعال، لأمكن التَّمانُعُ بينهما ؛ بأن يريد أحدهما حركة زيد مثلاً، والآخرُ سكونه، إذ كل منهما أمر ممكن في نفسه، وكذا تعلُّق الإرادة بكلِّ منهما، وحينئذٍ إما أن يحصل الأمران فيلزم اجتماع الضّدَّين، أو لا فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما، وهو أمارة الحدوث والإمكان لما فيه من شائبة الاحتياج، فالتعدُّد مستلزم لإمكان التَّمانُع، المستلزم للمحال، فيكون التعدد محالاً
His statement: (that if multiplicity were possible): that is, in essence, attributes, and actions, then mutual hindrance between them would be possible; such that one of them wills the movement of Zayd for example, and the other wills his stillness, since each of them is a possible matter in itself, and likewise the attachment of will to each of them. Then either both matters occur, necessitating the combination of two opposites, or they do not, necessitating the impotence of both or one of them, which is a sign of origination and possibility because of the shade of need in it. Thus multiplicity entails the possibility of mutual hindrance, which entails an impossibility, so multiplicity is impossible.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the central logical argument known as burhān al-tamānuʿ. The author explains that if multiplicity of gods were possible in essence, attributes, and actions, then it would be possible for them to mutually hinder each other. For example, one could will Zayd's movement and the other his rest, both being possible independently. In that case, either both wills are realized (impossible because opposites cannot coexist) or neither is realized (implying impotence). Impotence indicates contingency and need, which are incompatible with divinity. Therefore, the possibility of mutual hindrance follows from multiplicity, and since mutual hindrance leads to impossibility, multiplicity itself is impossible. This is a concise but rigorous proof of tawhid.

Key Terms
التعدد
التمانع
اجتماع الضدين
العجز
الحدوث
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance requires that multiple gods actually conflict.
Correction: Al-Sawi's argument is based on the logical possibility of conflict, not its actual occurrence. Even if gods never disagree, the possibility alone is enough to derive the contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what does the possibility of mutual hindrance entail?
A) That multiple gods exist
B) That either opposites combine or impotence occurs
C) That the universe is eternal
D) That God's will is limited

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of gods is impossible because it logically entails mutual hindrance, which leads to an absurdity (either combination of opposites or impotence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the possibility of two gods conflicting logically leads to impossibility, then why do you still sometimes act as though there are competing authorities in your life—your desires, people's opinions, and your own plans—rather than submitting wholly to the One whose will cannot be thwarted? How will you identify and dethrone one such false 'god' this week?
?
The proof shows that any sign of need or impotence disqualifies a being from divinity. Where in your heart do you still rely on created things to give you what only Allah can give? What would it cost you to trust Him alone for that need, starting today?
?
The argument hinges on possibility, not actual conflict. Are you waiting for a 'crisis of gods' before you take tawhid seriously? If the very possibility of another power is a sufficient threat to Allah's uniqueness, how does that reshape the way you view your whispered doubts and subtle shirk? What one step will you take to uproot them?

Answering the Objection and the Decisive Nature of the Proof
Al-Sawi refutes the objection that gods could agree, then affirms the proof as decisive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere possibility of conflict among gods is sufficient to negate polytheism, and why the proof from the Qur'an is considered decisive, not merely persuasive.

Core Idea
The possibility of divine conflict alone necessitates the impossibility of multiple gods.

After establishing the proof from mutual hindrance, al-Sawi now confronts an objection: could multiple gods simply agree and avoid conflict? He shows that the logical force of the proof does not depend on actual disagreement, but on the inherent possibility of it. He further strengthens the argument by citing al-Tabsira's stern warning and clarifying the meaning of the Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage before analysis.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إمكان التمانع' (possibility of mutual hindrance).
2
Identify the objection and its refutation
Find the objection that gods could agree without conflict. Then locate al-Sawi's refutation: that the mere possibility of conflict is enough to make polytheism impossible.
The refutation is based on logical necessity, not empirical observation.
3
Understand why the proof is decisive
Note that al-Sawi quotes from 'al-Tabsira' to emphasize that the argument is apodictic (قطعية), not merely rhetorical. Consider why anyone who treats it as merely persuasive nearly commits disbelief.
This underscores the theological seriousness of the proof.
4
Analyze the clarification of the verse
Examine the two scenarios for how multiple gods could create the heavens and earth: by combined power or by one alone. Understand why both lead to absurdities, and why 'فسدتا' means 'they would not exist'.
This connects the rational argument to the Qur'anic text.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the objection, refutation, quote, and clarification together form a decisive case against polytheism. Ask yourself why this proof is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ذكر اندفع ما يقال : إنَّه يجوز أن يتّفقا من غير تمانع، وحاصلُ الدَّفْع : أن الإمكان محال وإن لم يقع تمانع بالفعل . قوله: (وحاصل الدَّفع ... إلخ) : أي : فالآية حجة قطعية لا دليل إقناعي كما قيل، بل قال في «التَّبصرة» : (إِنَّ هذا القول كاد أن يكون كفراً) (۱) . : وإيضاح الآية : أنَّه لو تعدّد الإله لم تتكوّن السَّماوات والأرض؛ لأنَّ تكونهما إما بمجموع القدرتين أو بإحديهما، والكل باطل. أمَّا الأَوَّل : فلأنَّ شأن الإله كمال القدرة ، فإذا توجهت لشيء أبرزته. وأمَّا الآخر : فلما مرَّ ، فيلزم عجزه، فلا يوجد شيء من العالم، وعدم وجود العالم محال ؛ لأنَّه خلاف الحس والعيان، فيكون معنى (فسدتا): لم توجدا .
And by what was mentioned, what is said is repelled: that it is permissible for them to agree without mutual hindrance. And the gist of the repulsion: that possibility is impossible even if mutual hindrance does not actually occur. His statement: (And the gist of the repulsion... etc.): that is, so the verse is a decisive proof, not a persuasive proof as was said. Rather, he said in 'al-Tabsira': 'This statement almost amounts to disbelief.' And the clarification of the verse: that if there were multiple gods, the heavens and the earth would not come into being; because their coming into being is either by the combined two powers or by one of them, and both are invalid. As for the first: because the nature of the god is perfection of power, so when it is directed to something, it brings it forth. And as for the other: based on what preceded, so his impotence follows, so nothing of the world exists, and the non-existence of the world is impossible because it contradicts sense and observation. So the meaning of (they would have corrupted) is: they would not have come into being.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection and then reinforces the proof. First, al-Sawi notes that some might say gods could simply agree, avoiding conflict. He refutes this by stating that the mere possibility of conflict is enough to necessitate impossibility, even if actual conflict does not occur. The proof is based on what is logically possible, not what actually happens. Then he quotes from 'al-Tabsira' that anyone who says this proof is merely rhetorical almost commits disbelief, emphasizing its certainty. Finally, he gives an additional clarification: if multiple gods existed, the world would not have been created. The creation of the heavens and earth would require either combined power (but each god is perfect, so combined action is unnecessary) or the power of one alone (implying the others are impotent). Both lead to absurdities. Thus the verse's 'corruption' means non-existence of the world altogether. This drives home the point that the proof is decisive and based on clear logical and observable facts.

Key Terms
إمكان التمانع
حجة قطعية
دليل إقناعي
تكوين السماوات والأرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof of mutual hindrance requires actual conflict to disprove multiple gods.
Correction: Al-Sawi states that the very possibility (imkan) of mutual hindrance is enough to make polytheism impossible, even if no conflict occurs.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what does the word 'فسدتا' (they would have corrupted) mean in the verse?
A) They would have been destroyed
B) They would have been corrupt in governance
C) They would not have come into existence
D) They would have been damaged

Key Takeaway
The proof from mutual hindrance is decisive because the possibility of conflict, not actual conflict, logically necessitates the impossibility of multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you truly reflect on the proof that even the possibility of conflict among gods is impossible, does your heart submit completely to the oneness of Allah, or do you still attribute independent power to created beings in your daily fears and hopes? How can you train your heart to rely solely on the One whose power is absolute?
?
Al-Sawi warns that dismissing this proof as merely persuasive is almost disbelief. Are there areas where you treat the clear proofs of tawhid as abstract theology rather than as truths that should govern your actions? What would change in your life if you truly acted as if no one else shares an atom of Allah's lordship?
?
If the world's very existence depends on the impossibility of multiple gods, then your existence every moment witnesses to His oneness. How does this reality confront the moments when you act as if outcomes depend on your own efforts or on others' influence? What concrete step can you take today to translate this intellectual conviction into heartfelt trust in Allah alone?

The Two Principles of Tawhid
Al-Isfara'ini states the two principles of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational principles of Sunni tawhid as summarized by al-Isfara'ini: absolute transcendence (tanzih) and affirmation of attributes (ithbat al-sifat).

Core Idea
Tawhid rests on two pillars: God is unlike anything, yet He has real attributes.

Al-Isfara'ini, a major Ash'ari theologian, condenses the entire science of tawhid into two concise statements. These two principles – negating any similarity to creation and affirming divine attributes – are the bedrock of Sunni creed. This passage presents them clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two statements introduced by 'إحداهما' and 'ثانيهما'.
The passage begins with 'أجمع أهل الحقِّ' indicating scholarly consensus.
2
Identify the two principles
Identify the first principle: God is contrary to everything imaginable. Identify the second: His essence is not like any essence nor devoid of attributes.
The first is negation (tanzih), the second is affirmation (ithbat).
3
Understand why both are necessary
Reflect on why both principles are needed. The first prevents anthropomorphism, the second prevents emptying God of attributes (ta'til).
Most heretical sects err by overemphasizing one at the expense of the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني : (أجمع أهل الحقِّ على أنَّ ما قاله المتكلمون في التَّوحيد يرجع إلى كلمتين؛ إحداهما : اعتقاد أنَّ كلَّ ما تصوّر في الأذهان فالله بخلافه، ثانيهما اعتقاد أنَّ ذاته تعالى ليست مشبَّهة بذات، ولا خالية عن الصفات، وناهيك بسورة الإخلاص دليلاً؛ فإنَّها نفت أصول الكفر الثَّمانية ؛ الكثرة بمعنى : التّركيب والعدد، والنقص بمعنى: الاحتياج، والقلّة بمعنى البساطة، والعلة والمعلول، والشَّبيه والنَّظير، أمَّا الكثرة والعدد؛ فانتفاؤهما بقوله تعالى: (قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ)، والنَّقص والقلة بقوله: (اللهُ الصَّمَدُ)، والعلة والمعلول بقوله: (لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدْ)، والشَّبيه والنَّظير بقوله: (وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ).)
Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini said: 'The people of truth are agreed that what the theologians (mutakallimūn) have said about tawḥīd returns to two statements: one: the belief that everything that is pictured in minds, God is contrary to it; second: the belief that His essence, exalted, is not similar to any essence nor devoid of attributes. Sufficient is Surat al-Ikhlāṣ as evidence, for it negated the eight roots of disbelief: multiplicity meaning composition and number, deficiency meaning need, simplicity, cause and effect, likeness and counterpart. As for multiplicity and number: they are negated by His saying "Say: He is God, One"; deficiency and simplicity by His saying "God, the Eternal"; cause and effect by His saying "He begets not nor was begotten"; likeness and counterpart by His saying "And there is none comparable to Him."'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a citation from the Ash'ari theologian Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, summarizing the entire science of tawhid into two fundamental principles. The first principle is negative: God is utterly unlike anything imaginable (tanzih). The second is positive: God has real attributes (ithbat al-sifat) without being similar to creatures or being empty of attributes. Then the passage shows that Surat al-Ikhlas (Quran 112) is sufficient proof, as it negates eight types of disbelief: (1) multiplicity in the sense of composition and number, (2) deficiency/need, (3) simplicity (which in this context means being devoid of attributes), (4) cause and effect, and (5) likeness and counterpart. The surah's four verses correspond: 'Ahad' negates composition and number; 'al-Samad' negates need and simplicity (since al-Samad implies self-sufficiency and fullness of attributes); 'lam yalid wa lam yulad' negates causality; and 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' negates any equal or likeness. This demonstrates how the Qur'an concisely refutes all false conceptions of God.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التنزيه
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two principles are contradictory: if God is unlike anything, He cannot have attributes.
Correction: Both are affirmed simultaneously: God's attributes are real but not similar to created attributes. The principles complement each other.

Checkpoint
According to al-Isfara'ini, what does the first principle of tawhid state?
A) God is like the most perfect creation.
B) Everything pictured in minds, God is contrary to it.
C) God has no attributes at all.
D) God is similar to His creation in essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Isfara'ini's two principles show that tawhid requires both negating resemblance to creation and affirming real attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself picturing God in a way that resembles something in creation—a face, a hand, a location? How does the first principle of tawhid convict you today?
?
When you affirm that God has attributes like power and knowledge, do you secretly imagine them as limited like human attributes? How will you consciously avoid tashbih while still believing in real attributes?
?
If someone asked you, 'Do you believe God has attributes?' would your answer align with both principles? What is one practical step you can take this week to deepen your understanding of tawhid so that your worship is more sincere?

Surah al-Ikhlas Negates Eight Roots of Disbelief
Al-Isfara'ini shows how Surah al-Ikhlas negates eight roots of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Surah al-Ikhlas, according to al-Isfara'ini, refutes eight false conceptions of God.

Core Idea
One short surah demolishes eight roots of disbelief.

Having established the two principles, al-Isfara'ini points to Surah al-Ikhlas as a complete proof. This surah, with its four verses, systematically negates all false ideas about God. The passage shows how each verse refutes specific errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the eight roots
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the list of eight roots: multiplicity, deficiency, simplicity, cause, effect, likeness, counterpart (counted as eight).
The passage says ' eight roots' but lists seven terms; possibly cause and effect are two separate roots.
2
Match each root to a verse
Note how al-Isfara'ini assigns each set of roots to a specific verse: multiplicity/number to 'Ahad', deficiency/simplicity to 'al-Samad', cause/effect to 'lam yalid wa lam yulad', likeness/counterpart to 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad'.
3
Reflect on the sufficiency of the surah
Consider why this surah alone suffices as evidence for tawhid. It covers all major false conceptions about God concisely.
This demonstrates the Qur'an's linguistic and theological miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني : (أجمع أهل الحقِّ على أنَّ ما قاله المتكلمون في التَّوحيد يرجع إلى كلمتين؛ إحداهما : اعتقاد أنَّ كلَّ ما تصوّر في الأذهان فالله بخلافه، ثانيهما اعتقاد أنَّ ذاته تعالى ليست مشبَّهة بذات، ولا خالية عن الصفات، وناهيك بسورة الإخلاص دليلاً؛ فإنَّها نفت أصول الكفر الثَّمانية ؛ الكثرة بمعنى : التّركيب والعدد، والنقص بمعنى: الاحتياج، والقلّة بمعنى البساطة، والعلة والمعلول، والشَّبيه والنَّظير، أمَّا الكثرة والعدد؛ فانتفاؤهما بقوله تعالى: (قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ)، والنَّقص والقلة بقوله: (اللهُ الصَّمَدُ)، والعلة والمعلول بقوله: (لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدْ)، والشَّبيه والنَّظير بقوله: (وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ).)
Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini said: 'The people of truth are agreed that what the theologians (mutakallimūn) have said about tawḥīd returns to two statements: one: the belief that everything that is pictured in minds, God is contrary to it; second: the belief that His essence, exalted, is not similar to any essence nor devoid of attributes. Sufficient is Surat al-Ikhlāṣ as evidence, for it negated the eight roots of disbelief: multiplicity meaning composition and number, deficiency meaning need, simplicity, cause and effect, likeness and counterpart. As for multiplicity and number: they are negated by His saying "Say: He is God, One"; deficiency and simplicity by His saying "God, the Eternal"; cause and effect by His saying "He begets not nor was begotten"; likeness and counterpart by His saying "And there is none comparable to Him."'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a citation from the Ash'ari theologian Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, summarizing the entire science of tawhid into two fundamental principles. The first principle is negative: God is utterly unlike anything imaginable (tanzih). The second is positive: God has real attributes (ithbat al-sifat) without being similar to creatures or being empty of attributes. Then the passage shows that Surat al-Ikhlas (Quran 112) is sufficient proof, as it negates eight types of disbelief: (1) multiplicity in the sense of composition and number, (2) deficiency/need, (3) simplicity (which in this context means being devoid of attributes), (4) cause and effect, and (5) likeness and counterpart. The surah's four verses correspond: 'Ahad' negates composition and number; 'al-Samad' negates need and simplicity (since al-Samad implies self-sufficiency and fullness of attributes); 'lam yalid wa lam yulad' negates causality; and 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' negates any equal or likeness. This demonstrates how the Qur'an concisely refutes all false conceptions of God.

Key Terms
سورة الإخلاص
الكفر
الأحد
الصمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The surah only negates four things (one per verse).
Correction: Al-Isfara'ini identifies eight roots, with each verse covering two: e.g., 'Ahad' covers both composition and number.

Checkpoint
According to al-Isfara'ini, how many roots of disbelief does Surat al-Ikhlas negate?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Surah al-Ikhlas concisely refutes all false conceptions of God, proving its perfection as a declaration of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Surah al-Ikhlas daily, do you consciously acknowledge that you are affirming the negation of all false concepts about Allah—or has it become a mere habit? What would change if you paused on each verse to reflect on the specific falsehood it demolishes?
?
Consider the root 'al-qilla' (simplicity) that is negated by 'al-Samad'. Do you ever fall into the trap of thinking that Allah's attributes are 'simple' in the sense of being few or inadequate? How does the perfection of al-Samad challenge your heart to trust in His complete sufficiency?
?
The verse 'lam yalid wa lam yulad' negates cause and effect in Allah. Is there any area of your life where you treat created causes as if they have ultimate power, subtly attributing a form of causality to them rather than relying solely on Allah? How can you uproot that from your heart today?

Resolving the Apparent Redundancy in 'Laysa Kamithlihi Shay'
The author answers a famous objection about the redundant phrasing of Quran 42:11.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent redundancy in Quran 42:11 is resolved through two logical arguments.

Core Idea
The verse's apparent redundancy is a trap; its intended meaning directly negates any likeness.

The Quranic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' (There is nothing like unto Him) is a cornerstone of Islamic theology affirming God's absolute uniqueness. However, some have raised a grammatical objection: the combination of the prefix 'ka' (like) and the word 'mithl' (like) seems redundant, potentially implying a likeness that is then negated. In this brief passage, the author dismantles this objection with two clear, logical points, showing why the verse is both grammatically sound and theologically profound.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
The objection claims that combining 'ka' (meaning 'like') and 'mithl' (meaning 'like') is redundant, implying a 'like' that is then negated, which would be problematic for God.
This objection is famous among scholars and requires careful linguistic analysis.
2
Examine the First Refutation
The first refutation states that the intended meaning of the verse is to negate a like of God's essence directly, not to negate a 'like of a like'.
This pinpoints the actual target of negation.
3
Examine the Second Refutation
The second refutation argues that if the verse were negating a 'like of a like', that would actually affirm the existence of a 'like' (the original), which is impossible for God.
Negation of a duplicate presupposes the existence of the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة
في آية : (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ) سؤال مشهور، وهو أنَّ الجمع بين الكاف ومثل يوهم محالاً في حقه تعالى؛ لأنَّ الكاف بمعنى مثل، والنَّفي إنَّما تسلّط عليها، وهو باطل من وجهين:
أحدهما : أنَّ المقصود من الآية نفي مثل ذاته لا نفي مثل مثله
والآخر أنَّ نفي مثل المثل يقتضي إثبات المثل وهو محال.
Supplement: Concerning the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11), there is a famous question: the combination of the kāf and mithl (like) suggests something impossible regarding Him, because the kāf means 'like', and the negation applies to it. But this is invalid from two aspects: First: the intended meaning of the verse is the negation of a like of His essence, not the negation of a like of His like. Second: the negation of a like of a like necessitates affirming a like, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a brief supplemental note (tatimma) addressing a famous theological-linguistic objection to the Quranic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (42:11). The objection is that the verse uses both the kāf of comparison (ka-) and the word mithl (like), which are redundant since both indicate similarity. The objector claims that this construction could imply that there is a 'like' that is then negated, leading to an impossible implication. The author refutes this with two points: First, the verse intends to negate a like of God's own essence directly (i.e., nothing is like His essence), not to negate a 'like of a like'. Second, if it were negating a 'like of a like', that would actually affirm the existence of a 'like' (since you could only negate a duplicate of something that exists), which is impossible. Thus the objection is baseless, and the verse remains a clear affirmation of God's absolute uniqueness.

Key Terms
الكاف
مثل
النفي
المثل
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' is grammatically redundant or logically problematic.
Correction: The verse is grammatically sound; the apparent redundancy is resolved by understanding that the negation directly targets any likeness of God's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given to refute the objection about 'laysa kamithlihi shay'?
A) The verse is metaphorical
B) Negating a like of a like would affirm a like, which is impossible
C) The kāf is not meant for comparison
D) The verse only applies to created things

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' unequivocally negates any likeness of God, and the apparent redundancy is resolved by understanding its direct negation of the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Laysa kamithlihi shay', do you truly internalize that nothing—even your own mental image of Allah—resembles Him? What mental idol must you dismantle today?
?
The objection arises from linguistic redundancy. In your own life, how often do you overcomplain about Allah's attributes by relying on human comparisons? What change will you make tonight to worship Allah as He truly is?
?
This verse refutes any likeness of Allah. Is there any trait, person, or idea you have unconsciously compared to Allah? What step will you take to remove that from your heart?

The Two Principles of Tawhid
Al-Isfarayini states the two principles of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two fundamental principles of tawhid according to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini.

Core Idea
Tawhid boils down to two beliefs: God is unlike anything conceived, and His essence has unique attributes.

Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini distills all theological discussions on tawhid into two concise principles. This reduction cuts through complexity and provides a clear foundation for understanding divine oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the two principles.
2
Grasp the first principle
The first principle states that everything imagined by the mind is unlike God. This is the core of tawhid: absolute transcendence.
3
Grasp the second principle
The second principle affirms that God's essence is neither similar to any essence nor devoid of attributes. This balances transcendence with positive attributes.
4
Reflect on the combination
Notice how these two principles together prevent both anthropomorphism and stripping God of His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني : (أجمع أهل الحقِّ على أنَّ ما قاله المتكلمون في التَّوحيد يرجع إلى كلمتين؛ إحداهما : اعتقاد أنَّ كلَّ ما تصوّر في الأذهان فالله بخلافه ثانيهما اعتقاد أنَّ ذاته تعالى ليست مشبَّهة بذات، ولا خالية عن الصفات، وناهيك بسورة الإخلاص دليلاً؛ فإنَّها نفت أصول الكفر الثَّمانية ؛ الكثرة بمعنى : التّركيب والعدد، والنقص بمعنى: الاحتياج، والقلّة بمعنى البساطة، والعلة والمعلول، والشَّبيه والنَّظير، أمَّا الكثرة والعدد؛ فانتفاؤهما بقوله تعالى: (قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ) ، والنَّقص والقلة بقوله : (اللهُ الصَّمَدُ) ، والعلة والمعلول بقوله: (لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدْ) ، والشَّبيه والنَّظير بقوله : (وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ).
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said: 'The people of truth have agreed that what the theologians said about tawhid reduces to two statements: one of them is the belief that everything that is conceived in the minds is unlike God (fa-Allah bi-khilafihi); the second is the belief that His essence, exalted is He, is neither similar to any essence nor devoid of attributes. And suffice it with Surah al-Ikhlas as evidence, for it negates the eight roots of disbelief: multiplicity in the sense of composition and number, deficiency in the sense of need, scarcity in the sense of simplicity, cause and effect, and similarity and equivalent. As for multiplicity and number, their negation is in His statement: "Say: He is Allah, the One" (Qul huwa Allahu ahad); as for deficiency and scarcity: "Allah, the Eternal Refuge" (Allahu al-samad); as for cause and effect: "He begets not, nor is He begotten" (Lam yalid wa lam yulad); as for similarity and equivalent: "And there is none comparable to Him" (Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad).'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, a prominent Ash'ari theologian. It is a concise definition of tawhid (divine oneness) reduced to two fundamental beliefs: first, that everything conceived by the mind is completely different from God (i.e., God is not like any created thing, nor do created things resemble Him); second, that God's essence is neither similar to any other essence nor stripped of attributes (i.e., He has attributes but they are not like those of creation). Al-Isfarayini then presents Surah al-Ikhlas as a complete proof, claiming it refutes eight 'roots of disbelief.' He enumerates these eight: 1) multiplicity (kathrah) meaning composition and number; 2) deficiency (naqs) meaning need; 3) scarcity (qillah) meaning simplicity; 4) cause (illah); 5) effect (ma'lul); 6) similarity (shabih); 7) equivalent (nazir). He then matches each pair to verses of the surah: verse 'Qul huwa Allahu ahad' negates multiplicity and number; 'Allahu al-samad' negates deficiency and scarcity; 'Lam yalid wa lam yulad' negates cause and effect; 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' negates similarity and equivalent. The structure shows that the surah is a complete refutation of all false conceptions of God.

Key Terms
التوحيد
كلمتين
الأذهان
بخلافه
مشبهة
خالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawhid only means God is one in number.
Correction: Tawhid includes both transcendence of all mental images and affirmation of unique attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Isfarayini, what are the two principles of tawhid?
A) God's existence and His oneness
B) Everything conceived is unlike God, and God's essence is neither similar to nor devoid of attributes
C) God's power and will
D) God's mercy and justice

Key Takeaway
Tawhid is founded on two principles: God's absolute incomparability to any mental concept and His possession of unique attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you catch yourself forming a mental image? The first principle says God is beyond any conception. How has your imagination inadvertently committed tashbih? What specific steps will you take to purify your thoughts of God from any likeness?
?
The second principle affirms that God has attributes. Have you ever felt uncomfortable talking about God's anger or love because it seemed too human? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to strip God of His attributes? What attribute of God will you meditate on today, without fear of anthropomorphism?
?
Al-Isfarayini says this is the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq. Does your personal understanding of tawhid reduce to these two principles? If not, where is it lacking? Write down one concrete change you will make in your belief or practice as a result of this lesson.

Surah al-Ikhlas: The Proof of Tawhid
Al-Isfarayini proves tawhid from Surah al-Ikhlas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Surah al-Ikhlas refutes eight roots of disbelief and serves as a complete proof of tawhid.

Core Idea
Surah al-Ikhlas negates eight false conceptions about God in just four verses.

Al-Isfarayini claims that Surah al-Ikhlas is a sufficient proof for tawhid. He shows how each verse negates specific false beliefs, and together they cover all major misconceptions about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the proof section.
2
Identify the eight roots
The passage lists eight 'roots of disbelief' divided into four pairs: multiplicity/deficiency, scarcity/cause-effect, similarity/equivalent.
Note that 'scarcity' means simplicity here.
3
Match each verse to its negation
Assign each pair to the corresponding verse of Surah al-Ikhlas.
4
Reflect on completeness
Consider how these four verses cover all possible false conceptions.

Six Answers to the Objection on 'Laysa Kamithlihi Shay'
Six answers to a linguistic objection on Q.42:11

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous objection to the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (Q.42:11) and the six linguistic-theological answers that defend the absolute uniqueness of Allah.

Core Idea
The six answers show how Arabic rhetoric protects God's transcendence from a linguistic misreading.

The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' is a cornerstone of Islamic theology, but its precise wording has been scrutinized. The combination of 'ka' and 'mithl' seems to some to imply a contradiction. This passage presents a famous objection and supplies six sophisticated answers that demonstrate the subtlety of Arabic and the depth of theological reasoning. We will explore each answer and see how they collectively reinforce the absolute uniqueness of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its two initial refutations
Read the opening of the passage where the objection is posed. The author immediately gives two preliminary refutations showing the problem is not real. This sets the stage for the six detailed answers.
The objection claims that 'ka' (like) and 'mithl' (like) together create an odd meaning: 'nothing like His likeness'.
2
Read the first two answers (ka as extra and ka as emphasis)
The first answer says the 'ka' is extra (za'idah) without emphasis, so it is grammatically harmless. The second answer says 'ka' is for emphasis, meaning the negation itself is emphasized, not that it negates the 'likeness of a likeness'.
These two answers deal directly with the function of 'ka' in the sentence.
3
Examine answers 3 and 4 (mithl as mathal/attribute or as nafs/self)
The third answer reinterprets 'mithl' as 'mathal' (attribute) so the verse means 'nothing like His attribute'. The fourth takes 'mithl' to mean 'nafs' (self), as in a Qur'anic usage.
These shift the meaning of 'mithl' itself to avoid any implication of a likeness.
4
Explore the final two answers based on kinayah (metonymy)
The fifth and sixth answers treat the entire phrase as a metonymy (kinayah). The fifth argues that negating the 'likeness of a likeness' indirectly negates the 'likeness' itself via the principle of 'lazim' and 'malzum'. The sixth uses the Arabic idiom 'mithluka la yabkhalu' to show that the construction is an emphatic way of negating any likeness to God.
These are the most subtle answers, using advanced Arabic rhetorical concepts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة في آية : (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَىءٌ) سؤال مشهور، وهو أنَّ الجمع بين الكاف ومثل يوهم محالاً في حقه تعالى؛ لأنَّ الكاف بمعنى مثل، والنَّفي إنَّما تسلّط عليها، وهو باطل من وجهين: أحدهما : أنَّ المقصود من الآية نفي مثل ذاته لا نفي مثل مثله والآخر أنَّ نفي مثل المثل يقتضي إثبات المثل وهو محال. أجيب عنه بستة أجوبة : أحدهما : أنَّ الكاف زائدة لغير توكيد . والثَّاني : أنَّها مؤكّدة لنفي الشَّبيه ؛ أي: انتفى المثل انتفاء مؤكداً، لا أَنَّه من نفي المؤكِّد الذي هو مثل المثل ، حتّى يتوهم بقاء المثل . الثالث : أنَّ مثل بمعنى : المَثَل - بفتحتين - أي: الصِّفة . الرابع : أنَّه بمعنى نفس؛ نحو: (فَإِنْ ءَامَنُوا بِمِثْلِ مَا ءَامَنتُم بِهِ) . الخامس : أنَّه من باب الكناية، وفيها طريقان ثانيهما هو السَّادس، وتقرير أوَّلهما : أنَّ نفى مثل المثل أريد به نفي المثل ؛ لأنّ مثل المثل لازم للمثل، ونفي اللازم يدلُّ على نفي الملزوم، الثاني: أنَّها من باب مثلك لا يبخل، بمعنى: أنت لا تبخل فالقصد نفي مثله تعالى بأبلغ وجه، إذ هي أبلغ من الصَّريح؛ لتضمنها إثبات الشَّيء بدليله .
A supplement on the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Laysa kamithlihi shay'). There is a famous question: that the combination of the kaf and mithl implies impossibility with respect to Him, exalted, because the kaf means 'like' (mithl), and the negation (nafi) is only directed at it, and it (the implication) is invalid for two reasons: One: that the intended meaning of the verse is the negation of the likeness of His essence (mithl dhatihi), not the negation of the likeness of the likeness (mithl al-mithl). The other: that negating the likeness of the likeness would necessitate affirming a likeness, which is impossible. It [the question] is answered with six answers: One: that the kaf is extra (za'idah) without emphasis. Second: that it is for emphasizing the negation of likeness, i.e., the likeness is negated with emphasis, not that it is from the negation of the emphasized thing which is the likeness of the likeness, so that the continued existence of the likeness is imagined. Third: that 'mithl' means 'characteristic' (al-mathal, with two fatahs), i.e., attribute (al-sifah). Fourth: that it means 'self' (nafs), as in 'Then if they believe in the like of that which you believe' (fa-in amanu bi-mithli ma amantum bihi). Fifth: that it is from the category of metonymy (al-kinayah), and within it are two ways, the second of which is the sixth answer. The exposition of the first of them: that negating the likeness of the likeness intends the negation of the likeness because the likeness of the likeness is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the likeness, and negating the concomitant indicates the negation of the thing of which it is a concomitant (malzum). The second (way): that it is from the category of 'one like you does not act stingily' (mithluka la yabkhalu), meaning 'you do not act stingily,' so the intent is to negate His likeness, exalted, in the most emphatic manner, because it is more emphatic than the explicit statement, as it implies establishing the thing through its proof.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a theological-linguistic commentary on the famous verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (Q.42:11). The author first presents a well-known objection: the combination of 'ka' (like) and 'mithl' (like) seems to imply an impossibility, because 'ka' itself means 'like,' and the negation is applied to it, leading to the idea that something like a likeness is negated, which could be taken to mean that a likeness does exist but its likeness is negated. The author then refutes this objection in two initial ways: first, the intended negation is of any likeness to God's essence, not of the likeness of such a likeness; second, if it were negating the likeness of a likeness, that would imply the existence of a likeness, which is impossible. The author then provides six more detailed answers: (1) the 'ka' is extra (za'idah) and not for emphasis, so it is superfluous. (2) The 'ka' is for emphasis of the negation, i.e., the negation of any likeness is emphatic, not that it negates the emphasized thing (the likeness of a likeness). (3) The word 'mithl' here is read as 'mathal' (with two fatahs) meaning 'attribute' or 'description,' so the verse means 'nothing like His description.' (4) The word 'mithl' means 'self' (nafs), so it is like saying 'a self like Him' which is negated. (5 and 6) The construction is a metonymy (kinayah). The fifth answer itself contains two sub-answers: the first sub-answer argues that negating the 'likeness of a likeness' is a way of negating the 'likeness' itself, because the latter is a necessary concomitant of the former; negating the concomitant negates the original. The second sub-answer (the sixth overall) uses the Arabic idiom 'mithluka la yabkhalu' meaning 'one like you does not act stingily' but intended to mean 'you do not act stingily.' Similarly, 'laysa kamithlihi shay'' emphatically asserts that nothing is like God, more emphatically than a direct negation. This passage shows the depth of Arabic linguistic analysis in defending divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الكاف
مثل
المَثَل
الزائدة
التوكيد
الكناية
اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the six answers are independent of each other.
Correction: Actually the fifth contains two sub-answers making six, as the text clarifies: 'the fifth (answer) is that it is from the category of metonymy, and within it are two ways, the second of which is the sixth answer.'

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of the six answers in this passage?
A) To prove that God has a likeness
B) To refute the objection that the phrase 'kamithlihi' implies an impossibility
C) To explain the meaning of Surah al-Ikhlas
D) To argue that the kaf is always extra

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' is not problematic but rather a masterpiece of Arabic rhetoric that emphatically denies any likeness to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' in your prayers, do your heart and mind truly affirm that nothing—absolutely nothing—resembles Allah in any way? Or do unconscious images slip in? How can you train yourself to feel this transcendence every time you say it?
?
If someone were to question the clarity of this verse using the objection raised here, would you be able to confidently defend it? What truthful nuance have you learned that strengthens your conviction in the perfection of Allah's speech?
?
How often do you use comparisons to describe Allah, even casually? Let this lesson remind you that even the most subtle verbal association can diminish your tawhid. Today, commit to checking your words and thoughts, and ask Allah to purify your understanding of His incomparability.

Six Linguistic Interpretations of 'Kamithlihi' and Divine Oneness
Sawi enumerates six linguistic answers to the kaaf in 'kamithlihi' and links the argument to divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six linguistic interpretations that Ash'ari scholars use to defend the absolute transcendence of Allah in the verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Quran 42:11).

Core Idea
Six linguistic answers show that 'kamithlihi' does not imply any likeness to Allah.

This passage from Sawi's commentary addresses a subtle objection: the word 'kamithlihi' in the Quranic verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay' contains the letter kaaf, which could be thought to imply comparison. To safeguard divine oneness, scholars have proposed six distinct linguistic analyses. Sawi enumerates them systematically, showing how each interpretation reinforces the negation of any likeness to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the structure: a premise, six listed answers, and a concluding remark linking to tawhid.
The premise states that influence (tathīr) is only for Allah, which leads to the objection about 'kamithlihi'.
2
Identify each of the six answers
Go through the six answers one by one. For each, identify what linguistic move is being made: is the kaaf considered extra, emphatic, or part of a metonymy? Write a short label for each answer.
The answers are numbered in the passage: أحدهما, والثاني, etc.
3
Understand the metonymy (answers 5 and 6)
Focus on the fifth and sixth answers, which involve metonymy (kināyah). The fifth uses the logic of necessary and necessitated (lāzim and malzūm). The sixth uses a rhetorical expression like 'your like does not act miserly'. Understand how these effectively negate any likeness.
The phrase 'your like does not act miserly' means 'you do not act miserly' – it is a more emphatic negation.
4
Connect to the concluding remark
Read the last sentence: 'His saying (And when you know that He, exalted, must have oneness)...' This shows that the entire discussion on influence and the six answers is a corollary of tawhid. How does this reinforce the lesson?
The argument begins and ends with divine oneness.

الثالث : أنَّ مثل بمعنى : المَثَل - بفتحتين - أي: الصِّفة . الرابع : أنَّه بمعنى نفس؛ نحو: (فَإِنْ ءَامَنُوا بِمِثْلِ مَا ءَامَنتُم بِهِ (۱) . الخامس : أنَّه من باب الكناية، وفيها طريقان ثانيهما هو السَّادس، وتقرير أوَّلهما : أنَّ نفى مثل المثل أريد به نفي المثل ؛ لأنّ مثل المثل لازم للمثل، ونفي اللازم يدلُّ على نفي الملزوم، الثاني: أنَّها من باب مثلك لا يبخل، بمعنى: أنت لا تبخل فالقصد نفي مثله تعالى بأبلغ وجه، إذ هي أبلغ من الصَّريح؛ لتضمنها إثبات الشَّيء بدليله .
6
قوله : (وإذا علمت أنَّه تعالى يجب له الوحدانية) : أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ قوله : ( فالتأثير . .. إلخ ) : مفرَّع على وجوب الوحدانية له تعالى في الذات والصفات والأفعال.
(۱) سورة البقرة: (۱۳۷).
The acts of servants and the dispute therein. And when you know that He, exalted, must have oneness, then (influence) is invention and bringing into existence of things from nothing (is) not, i.e., it is not valid for anyone except the One. It is answered with six answers: First: that the kaaf is extra, not for emphasis. Second: that it emphasizes the negation of similitude, i.e., the likeness is negated with emphasis, not that it is from the negation of the emphasized which is the likeness of the likeness, so that the permanence of the likeness might be imagined. Third: that 'mithl' means 'mathal' (with two fat-hahs), i.e., the attribute. Fourth: that it means 'self', as in (Then if they believe in the like of that which you believe) [Quran 2:137]. Fifth: that it is from the category of metonymy, and in it there are two ways, the second of which is the sixth. The exposition of the first of them: that negating the likeness of the likeness intends negation of the likeness, because the likeness of the likeness is necessary for the likeness, and negation of the necessary indicates negation of the necessitated. The second: that it is from the category of 'your like does not act miserly', meaning: you do not act miserly, so the intent is to negate any like of Him, exalted, in the most eloquent manner, for it is more eloquent than the explicit because it entails proving the thing by its proof. His saying: (And when you know that He, exalted, must have oneness): he indicated by that that his saying (then influence... etc.) is a corollary of the necessity of oneness for Him, exalted, in essence, attributes, and actions.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine that only Allah has the power to create, and it addresses a linguistic objection to the Quranic verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay' (there is nothing like Him). The author begins by stating the proposition: if you know that Allah necessarily has oneness, then influence (tathir) – meaning invention and creation from nothing – is not valid for anyone except the One. This is the foundation for the discussion of human acts. An objection is then raised regarding the extra kaaf in 'kamithlihi', which might imply a likeness. The commentator provides six answers in order: (1) the kaaf is extra without emphasis; (2) it is an emphatic negation of similitude, not a negation of the emphasized; (3) the word 'mithl' here means 'sifah' (attribute); (4) it means 'nafs' (self) as in the Quranic example; (5) it is metonymy (kinayah) with two interpretations, the second being the sixth answer – the first interpretation negates the likeness by negating its necessary concomitant, the second is like saying 'your like does not act miserly' meaning 'you do not act miserly', thus negating any likeness in the most emphatic way. After the six answers, the author notes that the phrase 'wa idha `alimta' indicates that the statement about influence is a corollary of the necessity of oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. This reinforces that the entire argument about creation is built on tawhid.

Correction: The six linguistic answers show that the kaaf does not imply any comparison; it either is extra, emphatic for negation, or part of a metonymical expression that strengthens the negation.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay' does not allow any implication of likeness to Allah, as shown by six distinct linguistic interpretations that all reinforce His absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'laysa kamithlihi shay', do you feel the full weight of its negation, or does your mind still entertain unworthy images of Allah? Make a firm intention today to never let your thoughts reduce His majesty to created forms.
?
The six answers show that every possible linguistic loophole for resemblance is closed. Yet, have you allowed any resemblance to creep into your heart through false analogies or anthropomorphic ideas? Examine your inner speech about Allah and reject any notion that limits Him.
?
If the kāf in 'kamithlihi' is so carefully addressed, how much more should you guard your tongue and heart from assigning likeness to the Creator? Choose one area of your life where you have compared Allah to something created and repent from that thought right now.

The Sunni-Mu'tazili Debate on Divine Decree
Sawi narrates a debate illustrating God's absolute ownership and justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on divine decree as defended against Mu'tazili objections, centering on God's absolute ownership of all things.

Core Idea
God's absolute ownership renders His actions beyond human standards of fairness.

This passage presents a famous exchange between a Mu'tazili judge and a Sunni imam. The debate lays bare the core difference between the two schools: whether God's actions can be judged by human notions of justice. As you read, notice how each response reframes the question to affirm God's sovereignty over all occurrences.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Follow the back-and-forth: each statement is a response to the previous one.
The text ends mid-sentence; the Sunni's conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the key moves
Trace how the Sunni reframes the Mu'tazili's challenge from 'God is free from indecency' to 'nothing occurs in His dominion against His will.' Notice the shift in focus.
3
Analyze the final exchange
The Mu'tazili poses a dilemma about God withholding guidance. The Sunni's response, though cut off, contains the doctrinal key: what is God's is not owed to anyone.
This illustrates the Sunni principle of absolute divine ownership.
4
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Ask yourself: why does the Sunni insist that God's withholding of what is His cannot be evil? This sets the stage for later lessons on human acquisition of actions.
The Sunni affirms both divine will and human responsibility.

وحكي أنَّ القاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد المعتزلي قاضي قزوين دخل عند ابن عباد وزير المعزّ ، فرأى عنده الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفرايني إمام أهل السُّنَّة، فقال عبد الجبار : سبحان من تنزّه عن الفحشاء، ففهم السُّنِّيُّ مراده فقال سبحان من لا يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، فقال المعتزلي : أيريد ربُّك أن يعصى؟ فقال له السُّنِّيُّ : أيعصى ربُّنا قهراً عليه؟ فقال له المعتزليُّ : أرأيت إن منعني الهدى وقضى عليَّ بالرَّدى، أحسن إليَّ أم أساء؟ فقال السُّنْيُّ : إن منعك ما هو لك ؛ فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له؛
And it is narrated that the judge Abdul-Jabbar ibn Ahmad al-Mu'tazili, the judge of Qazwin, entered upon Ibn 'Abbad, the vizier of al-Mu'izz, and saw with him the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, the imam of the Sunnis. So Abdul-Jabbar said: 'Glorified is He who is free from indecency.' The Sunni understood his intent and said: 'Glorified is He in whose dominion nothing occurs that He does not will.' The Mu'tazili said: 'Does your Lord desire to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said to him: 'Is our Lord disobeyed coercively over Him?' The Mu'tazili said: 'Tell me: if He withholds guidance from me and decrees perdition upon me, has He done good to me or evil?' The Sunni said: 'If He withholds something that is yours, then He has done evil; but if He withholds something that is His, [then He has not].'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a narrated debate between a prominent Mu'tazili scholar, Abdul-Jabbar, and a Sunni imam, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. The Mu'tazili begins by declaring God's transcendence from indecency, implying that God cannot be the creator of evil actions. The Sunni responds by affirming that nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills, thereby asserting God's absolute sovereignty. The Mu'tazili then asks if God wills disobedience, and the Sunni counters by asking if God is disobeyed coercively, meaning that any disobedience happens only by God's permission. The Mu'tazili then raises a dilemma: if God withholds guidance and decrees perdition, is that good or evil? The Sunni's answer, though cut off, is that if God withholds something that belongs to the servant, it would be evil, but since everything belongs to God, His withholding is not evil. This illustrates the Sunni principle that God's actions are not subject to human concepts of fairness because He is the absolute owner of all.

Key Terms
سبحان (glorified)
فحشاء (indecency)
ملك (dominion)
إرادة (will)
هدى (guidance)
ردى (perdition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni position makes God the author of evil.
Correction: Sunnis distinguish between creation and acquisition: God creates all actions, but humans acquire them with free choice. The debate shows that God's actions are not judged by human standards of justice because He is the absolute owner.

Checkpoint
In the debate, what was the Sunni's implied answer to the Mu'tazili's question about withholding guidance?
A) God is always good regardless.
B) It is evil because guidance belongs to the servant.
C) It is not evil because everything belongs to God.
D) God cannot withhold guidance.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership means His actions are not subject to human standards of justice; whatever He does is just because He is the owner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty or loss, do you ever feel that God has wronged you? In light of the Sunni’s argument about ownership, what would be the correct response to that feeling?
?
The Mu'tazili assumed that withholding guidance is evil unless justified. Do you secretly hold God to a standard of fairness based on your own expectations? How can you submit to His absolute ownership even when it doesn't make sense to you?
?
If everything you have—your health, wealth, and even your faith—belongs to God, what right do you have to complain when He takes something away? What concrete attitude change does this demand from you today?

The Summary Proof for God's Negative Attributes
Al-Sawi outlines the summary proof for the five negative attributes using a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conditional 'if not' argument demonstrates that God must be described with the five negative attributes, and why the negation of any one would imply temporality.

Core Idea
Denying any negative attribute of God would entail temporality; therefore, He must possess them all.

Al-Sawi provides a concise proof that God necessarily possesses the five negative attributes: eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to creation, self-subsistence, and oneness. The proof hinges on a simple conditional: if He lacked any of these, He would be temporal and contingent. This lesson unpacks the logical flow of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument.
Note the conditional phrase 'لو لم يكن' (if He were not).
2
Identify the five negative attributes
The passage lists five negations: not eternal, not everlasting, similar to contingents, not self-subsistent, not one. List them and notice how they correspond to the standard negative attributes.
3
Understand the logical structure
The proof is a conditional: If He were not described with these, then temporality would follow. Since temporality is impossible for God, He must be described with them. Follow the reasoning from the conditional to the conclusion.
The commentator clarifies that 'in summary' means detailed proofs were given earlier.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why proving negative attributes this way is foundational: it safeguards God's transcendence and establishes that He is not like contingent beings.
This proof is concise but powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الإجمالي لاتصافه تعالى بالصفات السلبية ثمَّ أشار غفر الله له إلى برهان الصفات السلبية إجمالاً بقوله : لو لم يكن أي : إنَّما وجب اتّصافه بالصفات السلبيَّة لأنَّه لو لم یکن متّصفاً بها بأن كان غير قديم أو باق، أو كان مماثلاً للحوادث، أو غير قائم بنفسه، أو غير واحد فيما مرّ، (الزم) : حدوثه) تعالى عن ذلك. أما القِدَم : السنوسي في كتبه. ذكره. قوله : (إجمالاً) أي: وأمَّا تفصيلاً فقد تقدم دليل كلّ منها عند قوله : (أي : إِنَّما وجب اتصافه . . . إلخ) : أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ قوله : (لو لم يكن .... إلخ علة في الحقيقة لمحذوف واقع في جواب سؤال مقدر؛ قدره بقوله : إِنَّما وجب ... إلخ. قوله : متصفاً بها أي بهذه الخمسة بأن انتفى عنه الاتصاف ولو ببعضها. قوله : (بأن كان غير قديم أي فقط ومن باب أولى إذا كان غير متّصف بجميعها، فنفي أي واحد منها يلزم منه الحدوث، تعالى الله عنه. قوله : فيما مرَّ) : أي : في الذات والصفات والأفعال.
The summary proof for His Most High being qualified with the negative attributes. Then [the author], may God forgive him, pointed to the proof for the negative attributes in summary by his saying: 'If He were not' — i.e., He must necessarily be qualified with the negative attributes because if He were not qualified with them, such that He was not eternal or everlasting, or was similar to contingent things, or not self-subsistent, or not one in what has passed, then it would necessitate His temporality — exalted is He above that. As for eternity: al-Sanusi mentioned it in his books. His saying: 'in summary' — i.e., as for in detail, the proof for each has preceded in his saying: (i.e., He must necessarily be qualified...) etc.: He indicated by that that his saying 'If He were not... etc.' is in reality the cause for an omitted [answer] occurring in response to an implied question; he estimated it by his saying 'He must necessarily be... etc.' His saying: 'qualified with them' i.e., with these five, such that qualification with even some of them is negated. His saying: 'that He was not eternal' — i.e., only that, and a fortiori if He were not qualified with all of them, for negation of any one of them necessitates temporality, God is exalted above that. His saying: 'in what has passed' — i.e., in the essence, attributes, and actions.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage provides a summary proof for God's negative attributes. The author first states that the proof proceeds by the conditional 'if not' (لو لم يكن). The meaning is that God must necessarily be described with the negative attributes (الصفات السلبية), because if He were not described with them — for example if He were not eternal (قديم) or everlasting (باق), or if He were similar to contingent things (مماثل للحوادث), or not self-subsistent (قائم بنفسه), or not one (واحد) — then this would necessitate His temporality (حدوث). Since God is eternal, these negations must apply. The commentator then explains that 'in summary' means that the detailed proofs for each attribute were given earlier. He clarifies that the phrase 'if He were not' is the cause for an implied answer to an imagined question: 'Why must he be qualified with negative attributes?' The answer: 'Because if not, temporality would follow.' Then the commentator specifies that the five attributes are the ones listed, and that negation of even one of them implies temporality. The phrase 'in what has passed' refers to the categories of essence, attributes, and actions, where the principle of oneness applies.

Misconception: Negative attributes are arbitrary or merely human denials without rational basis.
Correction: The passage shows they are rationally necessary: if any were absent, temporality would follow.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God must possess all five negative attributes because lacking any one would entail temporality, which is impossible for the Eternal.

?

What fear in your heart prevents you from fully submitting to that truth? Spend ten minutes today in silent contemplation asking Allah to remove that veil.
?
