Proofs of God's Unlikeness and Oneness
Sanusi presents proofs for God's unlikeness and oneness with logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs: that God is unlike creation and that He is one, including the logical structure of the exceptive syllogism.

Core Idea
Resemblance implies creation; peer implies inability; both impossible for God.

This passage from a classic Ash'ari text presents two concise rational arguments for essential divine attributes. The first establishes that God is unlike any created thing, and the second proves His oneness. The commentary then unmasks the logical form of the second proof as an exceptive syllogism. By studying these proofs, you will see how Islamic theology uses clear logical reasoning to defend core beliefs about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two separate proofs and the commentator's glosses.
The passage contains two proofs and two clarifications.
2
Analyze the proof of unlikeness
Focus on the first argument: if God resembled any created thing, He would be originated. Why is that? Because resemblance would entail sharing the property of origination.
3
Analyze the proof of oneness
The second argument: if God had a peer (nazir), inability would follow. Inability implies being originated, which is impossible for God. Thus, no peer exists.
The commentator links 'inability' to an earlier proof called burhan al-tamānu'.
4
Understand the exceptive syllogism
The commentator explains that the second proof is actually an exceptive syllogism (istithna'iyya). Identify the premise ('if not described by these negations, origination would follow'), the excepted consequent ('origination is impossible'), and the implicit conclusion (He must be described by these negations).
The conclusion is omitted because it is clear from the exceptive form.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these two proofs together establish God's transcendence (unlike creation) and uniqueness (no partner). They are foundational for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المخالفة للحوادث : فلأنَّه لو ماثل شيئاً منها لكان حادثاً
وأما الوحدانية : فلأنه لو كان له نظير في ذاته أو صفاته للزم العجز، لما مرّ، وكل عاجز حادث، (وهو) أي: الحدوث عليه تعالى (محال) لا يقبل الثَّبوت عقلاً، وهذا إشارة إلى الاستثنائية، فهو في قوَّة قولنا : (لكن حدوثه محال).
قوله : (لما مر ) : أي : من برهان التّمانع .
قوله : (وهذا إشارة إلى الاستثنائيَّة :أي: لأنَّه ذكر المقدَّم بقوله : (لو لم يكن متّصفاً بها والتالي بقوله: (لزم حدوثه)، وحذف النتيجة لوضوحها، وهي عدم اتصافه بها محال؛ لأنَّ استثناء التّالي ينتج نقيض المقدَّم .
And as for being unlike created things: because if He resembled any of them, He would be originated. And as for oneness: because if He had a peer in His essence or attributes, inability would be necessary, as has passed, and every incapable is originated; (it) i.e., origination upon Him—Exalted is He—(is impossible) not accepting establishment rationally; and this is an indication of the exceptive syllogism, so it is in the force of our statement: 'but His origination is impossible.' His statement: 'as has passed' means: from the proof of mutual hindrance (al-tamānu'). His statement: 'and this is an indication of the exceptive syllogism' means: because he mentioned the premise with his statement 'if He were not described by them' and the consequent with his statement 'His origination would be necessary,' and omitted the conclusion due to its clarity, which is that His not being described by them is impossible; because excepting the consequent yields the contradictory of the premise.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage contains two separate proofs for two negative attributes. First, the proof that God is unlike created things (mukhalafat al-hawadith): if He resembled any created thing, He would be originated (hadith), which contradicts His eternity. Second, the proof of oneness (wahdaniyya): if He had a peer (nazir) in essence or attributes, inability ('ajz) would follow, and everything incapable is originated; thus, origination in God is impossible. The commentator then provides two glosses. The first gloss on 'as has passed' refers back to the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamānu') which earlier demonstrated the impossibility of two gods. The second gloss explains the logical form of the second proof: it is an exceptive syllogism (istithna'iyya) where the premise is 'if He were not described by them (i.e., the negative attributes), His origination would follow,' and the consequent is 'His origination is impossible,' so the conclusion—that He must be described by those attributes—is implicitly drawn. This logical analysis clarifies the structure of the argument.

Key Terms
المخالفة للحوادث
الوحدانية
العجز
التمانع
الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'peer' in the oneness argument refers only to a second god equal to Allah in power and attributes.
Correction: The 'peer' (nazir) includes any possible co-divinity that would entail imperfection; the proof is not merely quantitative but qualitative—any rival in essence or attributes would imply inability.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would follow if God had a peer (nazir) in essence or attributes?
A) God would be eternal.
B) Inability would follow, and every incapable is originated.
C) He would be similar to created things.
D) The premise would be contradicted.

Key Takeaway
God is unlike all creation and has no peer; both truths are proven rationally by arguing that their negations would entail impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof of oneness says a peer would imply inability. Do you ever act as if there are 'peers' to God in your life—people, possessions, or ambitions that you treat as having independent power? How can you practically affirm His sole sovereignty today?
?
The exceptive syllogism concludes that God's unlikeness and oneness are rationally necessary. If you were to defend these truths to a skeptic, would you rely on reason or revelation? Why? And does your daily life reflect a confidence in reason as a gift from God to know Him?

Divine Transcendence

Proof of Negative Attributes via Impossibility of Temporal Origination
Al-Sawi presents the cumulative proof for negative attributes via impossibility of temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cumulative rational proof that Allah's negative attributes follow from the impossibility of temporal origination (ḥudūth), and how this proof relies on the logical absurdities of infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr).

Core Idea
Temporal origination for Allah is impossible because it leads to infinite regress or circularity, both rationally absurd.

In this passage, al-Sawi presents a concentrated cumulative proof (burhān ijmālī) for Allah's negative attributes. He first restates the proofs for His difference from contingents and His oneness, then focuses on the central claim: temporal origination (ḥudūth) for Allah is rationally impossible. To prove this, he employs a conditional syllogism and shows that assuming ḥudūth would lead to either infinite regress or circularity—both established as impossible. This concise argument anchors the entire doctrine of divine transcendence in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Sawi uses a very compressed style here, referencing prior proofs.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that temporal origination (ḥudūth) for Allah is impossible. Find where this claim is explicitly made and how it is phrased.
The claim is presented as the conclusion of a conditional syllogism.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the impossibility
Understand the two consequences: if ḥudūth were possible, it would lead either to infinite regress (tasalsul) if the chain continues indefinitely, or to circularity (dawr) if it returns to the beginning. Both are impossible, so ḥudūth must be impossible.
This is a classic reductio argument in kalām.
4
Connect to negative attributes
See how the impossibility of ḥudūth establishes that Allah is unlike contingent things and is one without partner, thereby proving the negative attributes.
The passage references earlier proofs for each negative attribute individually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المخالفة للحوادث: فلأنه لو ماثل شيئاً منها لكان حادثاً وأما الوحدانية: فلأنه لو كان له نظير في ذاته أو صفاته للزم العجز، لما مر، وكل عاجز حادث، (وهو) أي: الحدوث عليه تعالى (محال) لا يقبل الثبوت عقلاً، وهذا إشارة إلى الاستثنائية، فهو في قوة قولنا : (لكن حدوثه محال). (فاستقم) تكملة، ولا تخلو عن فائدة. وإنما كان حدوثه تعالى محالاً لأنه يفضي) أي: يؤدي إلى التسلسل إن استمر العدد إلى ما لا نهاية له، وهو محال لما مر، (و) أي: أو يفضي إلى (الدور) إن لم يستمر؛ بأن رجع إلى الأول، فيكون الأول متأخراً، والمتأخر أولاً، (و) الدور (هو المستحيل المنجلي) أي: الظاهر، لظهور دليله، وقد مر. قوله : (لما مر): أي: من برهان التمانع. قوله: (وهذا إشارة إلى الاستثنائية: أي: لأنه ذكر المقدم بقوله: (لو لم يكن متصفاً بها والتالي بقوله: (لزم حدوثه)، وحذف النتيجة لوضوحها، وهي عدم اتصافه بها محال؛ لأن استثناء التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم. قوله: (ولا تخلو عن فائدة): أي: وهي أنه لما كان بصدد إقامة الدليل على ثبوت الصفات السلبية، وكان مقاماً تزل فيه الأقدام، وقد خالف في ذلك بعض فرق؛ نبه الطالب على الاستقامة على الطريق القويم. وإذا كان كل من التسلسل والدور محالاً فما أفضى إليهما - وهو الحدوث - يكون محالاً، وإذا كان الحدوث عليه تعالى محالاً ثبت اتصافه تعالى بالصفات السلبية على ما تقدم بيانه. وقد تقدم برهان كل صفة على حدتها تفصيلاً أيضاً عند ذكرها، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لهذا وما كنا لنهتدي لولا أن هدانا الله. قوله : (فما أفضى إليهما أي بالوسائط كما هو معلوم من تقرير البرهان. قوله : (وقد تقدَّم برهان كلِّ صفة) : أي : في الشارح . قوله: (والحمد لله الذي هدانا لهذا اقتباس من الآية الكريمة المحكية عن أهل الجنة؛ إشارة إلى عظم نعمة المعرفة بالله تعالى، إذ هي جنة الشهود المعجلة لأولياء الله تعالى في الدنيا، فمن أجل ذلك حمد بحمد أهل الجنة.
And as for being different from contingent things: because if He resembled any of them, He would be contingent. And as for oneness (waḥdāniyya): because if He had an equal in His essence or attributes, impotence (ʿajz) would necessarily follow, due to what has passed, and every impotent thing is contingent. And (that) meaning: temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) upon Him, exalted, is (impossible) not acceptable of establishment rationally. And this is an indication to the conditional syllogism (al-istithnā'iyya). So it is in the force of our saying: 'But His temporal origination is impossible.' (Be upright) is a completion, and it is not without benefit. And the reason why His temporal origination, exalted, is impossible is that it leads to infinite regress (al-tasalsul) if the number continues ad infinitum, and that is impossible due to what has passed, or it leads to circularity (al-dawr) if it does not continue, by returning to the first, so the first would be later and the later first. And circularity is the manifest impossibility, due to the clarity of its proof, and it has passed. He said: ('due to what has passed') meaning: from the proof of mutual exclusion (burhān al-tamānuʿ). He said: ('And this is an indication to the conditional syllogism') meaning: because he mentioned the premise with his saying: 'If He were not characterized by them' and the consequent with his saying: 'His temporal origination would necessarily follow,' and he omitted the conclusion due to its clarity, which is that His non-characterization by them is impossible; because the negation of the consequent yields the negation of the premise. He said: ('And it is not without benefit') meaning: that it is that when he was about to establish proof for the establishment of the negative attributes, and it was a station where feet slip, and some sects disagreed concerning that; he alerted the student to uprightness upon the straight path. And since both infinite regress and circularity are impossible, what leads to them—which is temporal origination—must be impossible. And if temporal origination upon Him, exalted, is impossible, then His being characterized by the negative attributes is established according to what has preceded of its explanation. And indeed the proof of each attribute separately in detail has also preceded when mentioning them. And praise be to Allah who guided us to this, and we would not have been guided unless Allah guided us. He said: ('So what leads to them') meaning: by intermediaries, as is known from the exposition of the proof. He said: ('And indeed the proof of each attribute has preceded') meaning: in the commentary. He said: ('And praise be to Allah who guided us to this') is a quotation from the noble verse narrated about the people of Paradise; an indication of the greatness of the blessing of knowledge of Allah, exalted, for it is the paradise of witnessing hastened for the saints of Allah, exalted, in this world; therefore he praised with the praise of the people of Paradise.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative proof (burhān ijmālī) for Allah's negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya), focusing on the impossibility of temporal origination (ḥudūth) for Him. The author proceeds argument by argument. First, he restates the proofs for difference from contingents and oneness: if Allah resembled contingents, He would be contingent; if He had an equal, impotence would follow, and impotence implies contingency. He then identifies the core claim: temporal origination (ḥudūth) for Allah is impossible. This is presented in the form of a conditional syllogism (istithnā'iyya): if a certain condition holds, then a consequence follows; the consequence is negated, so the condition is negated. The phrase 'Be upright' (fastaqim) is an exhortation to the student, indicating the importance of this demonstration. The proof that ḥudūth is impossible is given via two absurd consequences: if the chain of causes were infinite, there would be infinite regress (tasalsul); if it were finite, it would lead to circularity (dawr) because the first would depend on the last. Both are impossible, as previously established (e.g., in the proof of tamānuʿ). Therefore, what leads to them—i.e., ḥudūth—must also be impossible. Thus, Allah's existence is eternal without temporal origination, and the negative attributes are established. The glosses clarify that the proof of tamānuʿ is referenced, explain the logical structure of the conditional syllogism, and note that the exhortation is to keep students on the right path against dissenters. The concluding doxology quotes the Quranic verse of Paradise, emphasizing the grace of this knowledge. The final glosses clarify that the earlier proof is found in the commentary (sharḥ).

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
دور (dawr)
استثنائية (istithnā'iyya)
برهان التمانع (burhān al-tamānuʿ)
صفات سلبية (ṣifāt salbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that ḥudūth leads to tasalsul or dawr is itself dependent on unproven assumptions.
Correction: In fact, the premises are established earlier via the proof of mutual exclusion (tamānuʿ) and other rational arguments; the reasoning here is cumulative and builds on prior proofs, as the text notes by saying 'due to what has passed'.

Checkpoint
What two logical absurdities does the author use to prove that temporal origination (ḥudūth) for Allah is impossible?
A) Contradiction and impossibility
B) Infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr)
C) Mutual exclusion (tamānuʿ) and priority
D) Dependence and contingency

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of temporal origination for Allah is rationally proven by the absurd consequences of infinite regress and circularity, thereby establishing His negative attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you find yourself doubting the rational proof? Examine your heart: do you rely more on scriptural assertion or rational demonstration for your faith in Allah's transcendence? Commit to strengthening your understanding of these proofs so that your faith is built on both revelation and reason.

Has this knowledge truly transformed your worship and awe of Him, or has it remained abstract? Ask yourself: Do I perceive the study of these theological proofs as a form of worship that brings me closer to Allah, or do I treat it as mere intellectual exercise? Resolve today to approach each lesson as an act of devotion.

The Names al-Ṭāhir, al-Quddūs, al-Rabb, al-ʿAlī and Divine Transcendence
The commentator enumerates divine names of transcendence and denies corporeality from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine names al-Ṭāhir, al-Quddūs, al-Rabb, and al-ʿAlī affirm God's absolute transcendence and how the commentary denies indwelling, direction, and composition.

Core Idea
God is absolutely transcendent, free from place, direction, and composition.

This passage from the commentary on al-Kharīda explains several divine names that emphasize God's purity and transcendence. The author then explicitly denies any notion of God being confined to places, having direction, or being composed of parts. These denials are central to the Ashʿarī understanding of tanzīh.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the concise style of the commentary.
2
Understand the four divine names
Identify al-Ṭāhir, al-Quddūs, al-Rabb, and al-ʿAlī and note their meanings as given in the commentary.
Each name highlights an aspect of transcendence.
3
Identify the three denials
The passage denies ḥulūl (indwelling), jiha (direction), and ittiṣāl (connection in essence). Understand what each means.
These denials safeguard God's transcendence.
4
Reflect on the glosses
The glosses clarify grammatical and conceptual points, such as the meaning of 'great transcendence' and 'permeation'.
These details deepen understanding of the denials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان في بعض الأسماء والتنزيهات وهو (والطاهر) أي : المنزه عن كل ما لا يليق به، (القدوس) من القدس، الظهر؛ أي: العظيم التنزيه عن كل نقص، (والرب) أي : المالك ومربي الخلائق (العلي) أي: المرتفع القدر، المبرأ عن كل عيب. An exposition on some of the names and declarations of transcendence, and He is (al-Ṭāhir) meaning: the One purified from everything that is not befitting to Him, (al-Quddūs) from holiness, al-ẓahr? meaning: the great transcendence from every imperfection, (al-Rabb) meaning: the Owner and the Nurturer of creatures, (al-ʿAlī) meaning: the elevated in rank, the One free from every defect. (Munazzah) meaning: He is transcendent and purified (from al-ḥulūl) in places, or the indwelling of permeation, like the permeation of water in a green branch, (and from al-jiha) for anything, so it is not said that He is above a body, nor below it, nor to its right, nor to its left, nor behind it, nor in front of it. (And) transcendent from (al-ittiṣāl) in the essence. His statement: (meaning: the great transcendence) is from the attribution of the adjective to the described: i.e., the great transcendence. His statement: (and the Nurturer of creatures) meaning: the One who develops them little by little to that which He willed. His statement: (the One free from every defect) is an interpretation of what precedes. His statement: (meaning: He is transcendent) indicates that His word (munazzah) is a predicate of a deleted subject. His statement: (or the indwelling of permeation) meaning: in things such that it permeates every part of them. His statement: (al-ittiṣāl in the essence) meaning: that He be composed of parts that are connected to each other.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition of several divine names that emphasize God's absolute transcendence (tanzīh). The commentator first lists the names: al-Ṭāhir (the Pure), al-Quddūs (the Holy), al-Rabb (the Lord), and al-ʿAlī (the High). Each is explained: al-Ṭāhir is purified from all that does not befit Him; al-Quddūs indicates great holiness; al-Rabb means Owner and Nurturer; al-ʿAlī is elevated in rank and free from defect. Then the commentator turns to the explicit denial of three forms of immanence: (1) al-ḥulūl (indwelling) in places, and specifically a type of permeation (suryān) like water in a branch; (2) al-jiha (direction), negating any spatial relation such as above, below, left, right, front, or back; (3) al-ittiṣāl (connection) in the essence, meaning that God is not composed of interconnected parts. The glosses further clarify: the 'great transcendence' is an adjective-noun construct; 'Nurturer' means gradual development according to His will; 'free from defect' is interpretive; the word 'munazzah' is a predicate for an implicit subject; the permeation is explained as pervading every part; and 'connection in essence' means being a composite. This passage firmly establishes the Ashʿarī position of tanzīh—God is absolutely simple, non-spatial, and incorporeal.

Key Terms
الطاهر
القدوس
الرب
العلي
التنزيه
الحلول
السريان
الجهة
الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying direction (jiha) from God implies He does not exist or is nothing.
Correction: Denying direction affirms that God exists without occupying space or having a location; He is not subject to spatial relations.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT denied about God in this passage?
A) Indwelling in places (ḥulūl)
B) Having direction (jiha)
C) Having a body (jism)
D) Having attributes of perfection

Key Takeaway
The divine names affirm God's absolute transcendence, and the commentary explicitly denies that He dwells in places, has direction, or is composed of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your heart, do you unconsciously assign a direction or location to Him? This passage commands you to purify your concept of Allah from such imagination. What specific habit will you adopt to train your mind to think of Allah without spatial limitations?
?
The name al-Rabb means the One who nurtures you gradually towards what He wills. In what area of your life have you been impatient with Allah's gradual development? Will you accept His timing and trust that He is bringing you to your best end?
?
If Allah is truly transcendent and not subject to space, then His closeness to you is not physical but real—through knowledge, power, and response. How does your duʿāʾ reflect this reality? Are you content with a non-physical closeness, or do you secretly yearn for a tangible god? Commit to one change in how you call upon Him.

God's Transcendence Beyond Space and Direction
Hashiyah denies two forms of spatial limitation: indwelling and direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology negates both indwelling (ḥulūl) and direction (jihah) from God, and what specific forms of indwelling are rejected.

Core Idea
God is not inside anything, nor contained by any direction.

The hashīyah writer elaborates on a key line from al-Kharīda, affirming that God is transcendent (munazzah) and purified from two types of spatial limitation: indwelling in physical places or in a permeating manner, and being confined to any direction. This passage clarifies that God is not subject to the categories that apply to physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Then read the translation to understand the meaning. Notice the two key negations: ‘an al-ḥulūl and ‘an al-jihah.
Pay attention to how the author uses the word 'munazzah' as a predicate.
2
Understand the two types of indwelling
The passage distinguishes indwelling in places from indwelling of permeation (ḥulūl al-saryān). Reflect on why both are rejected—physical occupancy and immanent permeation are both incompatible with God's transcendence.
The example of water permeating a green branch illustrates how permeation would involve being in every part.
3
Reflect on negation of direction
The passage lists six directions (above, below, right, left, behind, front) and denies them all for God. Consider what this implies: God is not located in space at all.
This does not mean God is non-existent; He transcends spatial categories entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(منزّه) أي : هو منزّه ومطهر (عن الحلول) في الأمكنة، أو حلول السَّريان، كسريان الماء في العود الأخضر (والجهة) لشيء، فلا يقال : إنَّه فوق الجرم ولا تحته ولا يمينه ولا شماله، ولا خلفه ولا أمامه .

(Munazzah) meaning: He is purified and cleansed (from indwelling) in places, or indwelling of permeation, like the permeation of water in a green twig (and from direction) to anything, so it is not said: He is above a body nor below it, nor to its right nor its left, nor behind it nor in front of it. His saying: (meaning: He is purified) he indicated by that that His saying: (Munazzah) is a predicate of an omitted subject. His saying: (or indwelling of permeation) meaning: in things such that it permeates every part of them.

Commentary
This passage denies two main forms of anthropomorphism: al-ḥulūl (indwelling) and al-jihah (direction). It states that God is transcendent (munazzah) and purified from indwelling in physical places, and from a kind of indwelling that involves permeation, like water seeping into a green branch. God is also free from having a specific direction—we do not say He is above, below, right, left, behind, or in front of any body. The hashīyah clarifies that the word 'munazzah' is a predicate of an omitted subject (i.e., 'He is munazzah'), and that 'indwelling of permeation' means something that pervades every part of an object.

God is absolutely free from spatial attributes—He does not dwell in anything nor is He limited to any direction.

Negation of Divine Connection and Separation
Denial that God can be described as connected or separate from the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be described as connected to or separate from creation, and why such categories belong only to contingent beings.

Core Idea
God is beyond the categories of connection and separation because they apply only to contingent things.

In this passage, the author argues that describing God as 'connected' or 'separate' is inappropriate because these are attributes of created, contingent things. By understanding the radical difference between the Creator and creation, we see that such physical categories do not apply to the Divine. The lesson establishes the principle of divine transcendence over spatial relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the ellipsis after 'in essence'.
2
Identify the two denied terms
The passage denies both 'connection' (itiṣāl) and 'separation' (infiṣāl). Make sure you know what each means.
These are opposite relational categories.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reason given is that connection and separation are attributes of contingent things. Since God is not contingent, they do not apply.
Contingent means created and dependent.
4
Reflect on the world's insignificance
The world, despite its apparent greatness, is like nothing before God's power. This underscores the absurdity of applying physical categories to God.
This is a spiritual perspective, not a scientific one.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
Ask yourself: What does this teach us about how we should think about God? Avoid reducing Him to spatial or physical concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) منزه عن (الاتصال) في الذات، ...
أو بالغير، وعن (الانفصال فلا يقال : إنَّه متّصل بالعالم ولا منفصل عنه ؛ لأنَّ هذه الأمور من صفات الحوادث، والله ليس بحادث، وقد تقدَّم أنَّ العالم وإن عظم في نفسه فهو في جانب باهر قدرته كأنه ليس بشيء، فكيف يكون العلي الكبير الغني القدير حالاً أو متصلاً، أو منفصلاً في شيء حقير فقير، هو في نفسه عدم. قال العارف ابن عطاء الله في «الحكم» : (أيا عَجَبا كيف يظهر الوجود في العدم، أم كيف يثبت الحادث مع من له وصف القِدَم؟) انتهى سبحانه قد دلت على وجوب وجوده ،آیاته، وشهدت بوحدانيته مصنوعاته، ...
قوله : (أو بالغير) : أي : فليس متّصلاً بالعالم بحيث يكون حالاً أو سارياً فيه .
قوله : (كيف يظهر الوجود أي صاحب الوجود الواجب، وهو وجود الله تعالى. وقوله : (في (العدم أي في صاحبه، وهو ما سواه تعالى. قوله : (أم كيف يثبت الحادث أي على سبيل الاتصال والانفصال، وهو ما سواه تعالى. وقوله : (مع من له وصف القدم) : أي : وهو الله تعالى.
قوله : (سبحانه قد دلت على وجوب وجوده . . . إلخ): هذا نتيجة ما ...
(And) He is transcendent from (connection) in essence, ... or with other than He, and from (separation), so it is not said: He is connected to the world nor separate from it; because these matters are attributes of contingent things, and God is not contingent. It has already been mentioned that the world, though great in itself, is in the face of His overwhelming power as if it were nothing. So how can the Exalted, the Great, the Self-Sufficient, the All-Powerful be indwelling, or connected, or separate in a lowly, poor thing that is itself non-existence? The gnostic Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh said in al-Ḥikam: 'O wonder! How can existence appear in non-existence? Or how can the contingent be established with the One who has the attribute of eternity?' End. Glory be to Him, His signs have indicated the necessity of His existence, and His creations have witnessed His oneness. ... His saying: (or with other than He) meaning: He is not connected to the world such that He is indwelling or permeating it. His saying: (How can existence appear) meaning the owner of necessary existence, which is the existence of God Most High. And His saying: (in non-existence) meaning in its owner, which is everything other than Him. His saying: (Or how can the contingent be established) meaning in the manner of connection and separation, and it is everything other than Him. And His saying: (with the One who has the attribute of eternity) meaning: He is God Most High. His saying: (Glory be to Him, His signs have indicated the necessity of His existence... etc.): This is a result of what ...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage denies that God is composite (ittiṣāl) or separable (infiṣāl). It argues that connection and separation are properties of contingent things, and since God is not contingent, such categories do not apply. The world, however great, is insignificant before God's power, so it is absurd to think of the Exalted God being connected to or separate from a lowly contingent being that is essentially non-existent. The passage then quotes Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh's saying of wonder at how existence (God) appears in non-existence (creation), and how the contingent can be established with the Eternal. It concludes that God's signs prove His necessary existence and His works witness His oneness. The hashīyah clarifies that 'with other than He' means God is not connected to the world by indwelling or permeation, and it breaks down the quote: 'existence' means God, 'non-existence' means all besides Him, 'the contingent' means all besides Him, and 'the One who has eternity' is God. The commentary states that 'Glory be to Him...' is the result of the preceding argument.

Correction: The passage does not deny God's knowledge or power; it denies physical connection and separation, which are spatial categories.

Ibn Ata'illah's Wisdom on Contingency and Eternity
Explanation of Ibn Ata'illah's saying on the contingency of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Ata'illah's aphorism about existence appearing in non-existence and how it reinforces divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The contingent cannot stand before the Eternal; all creation is non-existence in comparison.

This lesson unpacks a profound saying by the gnostic Ibn Ata'illah al-Iskandari, marveling at how the Necessary Existence (God) manifests in the realm of non-existence (creation). The hashīyah clarifies each part of the saying and concludes that God's signs prove His necessary existence and oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the quote from Ibn Ata'illah
Read the Arabic quote and its translation. Ponder its meaning before reading the commentary.
The quote is a poetic expression of wonder.
2
Understand the hashīyah's breakdown
The hashīyah explains each term: 'existence' means God, 'non-existence' means creation, etc.
This clarifies the intended meaning behind the poetic language.
3
Reflect on the contrast
The contingent (creation) is established only with the Eternal (God). This highlights total dependence.
Creation has no independent existence.
4
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's signs and creations testify to His necessary existence and oneness.
This is the result of the reasoning.

Divine Transcendence

God is Not Connected to nor Separated from the World
Author denies connection/separation for God, citing Ibn 'Ata' Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be described as connected to or separated from the world, and how this affirms His absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
Connection and separation are attributes of contingents; God is beyond such categories.

This passage builds on the principle that the world is contingent and non-existent in itself compared to God's overwhelming power. The author denies that God can be described as connected to or separated from the world, because such categories belong only to contingent beings. He reinforces this with a profound aphorism from the gnostic Ibn 'Ata' Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's words sink in.
Note the use of technical terms like اتصال and انفصال.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the main claim: God is not connected nor separated. Then see why: because these are attributes of contingents, and God is not contingent.
The author also mentions the world's insignificance before God's power.
3
Reflect on Ibn 'Ata' Allah's quote
Consider the rhetorical questions: How can existence appear in non-existence? How can the contingent stand with the Eternal? What do they tell us about God's transcendence?
This quote reinforces the argument with poetic depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بالغير، وعن (الانفصال فلا يقال : إنَّه متّصل بالعالم ولا منفصل عنه ؛ لأنَّ هذه الأمور من صفات الحوادث، والله ليس بحادث، وقد تقدَّم أنَّ العالم وإن عظم في نفسه فهو في جانب باهر قدرته كأنه ليس بشيء، فكيف يكون العلي الكبير الغني القدير حالاً أو متصلاً، أو منفصلاً في شيء حقير فقير، هو في نفسه عدم.
قال العارف ابن عطاء الله في «الحكم» : (أيا عَجَبا كيف يظهر الوجود (1) في العدم، أم كيف يثبت الحادث مع من له وصف القِدَم؟) انتهى
وقوله : (أو بالغير) : أي : فليس متّصلاً بالعالم بحيث يكون حالاً أو سارياً فيه .
قوله : (كيف يظهر الوجود أي صاحب الوجود الواجب، وهو وجود الله تعالى.
وقوله : (في (العدم أي في صاحبه، وهو ما سواه تعالى. قوله : (أم كيف يثبت الحادث أي على سبيل الاتصال والانفصال، وهو ما سواه تعالى.
وقوله : (مع من له وصف القدم) : أي : وهو الله تعالى.
Or with another, and from separation, so it is not said: He is connected to the world nor separated from it, because these matters are attributes of contingents, and God is not contingent. And it has preceded that the world, even if it is great in itself, is in the face of His overwhelming power as if it were nothing. So how could the Most High, the Great, the Self-Sufficient, the Almighty be a state or connected or separated in a lowly, poor thing that is in itself non-existence? The gnostic Ibn 'Ata' Allah said in al-Hikam: 'O marvel! How can existence appear in non-existence? Or how can the contingent be established with the One who has the attribute of pre-eternity?' End. His saying: 'Or with another' means: He is not connected to the world such that He is a state or pervading in it. His saying: 'How can existence appear' means the possessor of necessary existence, which is the existence of God Most High. His saying: 'In non-existence' means in its possessor, which is other than Him Most High. His saying: 'Or how can the contingent be established' means in the way of connection and separation, and it is other than Him Most High. His saying: 'With the One who has the attribute of pre-eternity' means: He is God Most High.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a proof of divine transcendence. It begins by stating that God cannot be described as connected to or separated from the world, because connection and separation are attributes of contingent beings, and God is not contingent. The author then reinforces this with a quote from Ibn 'Ata' Allah, which rhetorically questions how the Eternal can appear in the contingent. The commentary then unpacks each phrase: 'or with another' denies that God is a state pervading the world; 'existence' refers to God's necessary existence; 'non-existence' refers to all other things; 'the contingent' also refers to all other things; 'the One who has the attribute of pre-eternity' is God. The overall argument is that God's transcendence precludes any relational category like connection or separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying connection and separation implies God is non-existent or absent.
Correction: The author clarifies that these categories apply only to bodies and contingents; God is beyond them, so denying them does not imply absence but rather affirms His transcendence.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn 'Ata' Allah marvels that the contingent can be established with the Eternal. Do you live with the awe of this reality, or have you become comfortable with the paradox? What would change if you truly felt your contingency before Him?

The Mu'tazila's Literalism and the Two Orthodox Responses
Author explains the Mu'tazila's confusion and the two orthodox responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila's literalist approach led to false beliefs about God, and how both the salaf (tafwid) and khalaf (ta'wil) correctly upheld divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Literalism without tanzih leads to error; salaf and khalaf both uphold tanzih via different methods.

This passage explains why some groups (the Mu'tazila) fell into error by clinging to literal meanings of scriptural texts while neglecting God's transcendence. It then presents two orthodox responses: the early scholars (salaf) consigned the meanings to God, while later scholars (khalaf) provided specific interpretations to refute deviant views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the problem
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the Mu'tazila took literal meanings of texts without considering God's transcendence, leading them to claim direction or corporeality.
The passage lists two mistaken beliefs: direction (jiha) and corporeality (jismiyya).
2
Understand the salaf's response: tafwid
The salaf (early scholars, including the four imams) responded by affirming God's transcendence and consigning the interpretation of anthropomorphic texts to God, considering it the safer path.
Tafwid means leaving the meaning to God, as the Quran says, 'None knows its interpretation except Allah.'
3
Understand the khalaf's response: ta'wil
The khalaf (later scholars) provided specific interpretations, e.g., 'hand' as power and 'face' as essence, to invalidate the misguided doctrines and guide those with limited understanding.
Ta'wil is a legitimate method used when needed to counter false beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (سبحانه قد دلت على وجوب وجوده . . . إلخ): هذا نتيجة ما قبله؛ أي: وحيث علمت ممَّا تقدَّم اتّصافه تعالى بتلك الصفات، فهو سبحانه قد دلت ... إلخ، وفي الكلام حذف الواو مع ما عطفت؛ أي: وتنزيهه عن النقائص، وإنَّما قلنا ذلك؛ ليصح ترتب قوله: (واشتبه الأمر .... إلخ عليه ؛ لأنَّه لا يترتب إلا على التنزه على النَّقائص، فتدبَّر.
قوله : ( واشتبه الأمر على أقوام) : أي : وهم المعتزلة.
وقوله : ( وقوفاً علَّة لما قبله؛ أي اختلط الأمر عليهم من أجل وقوفهم . . . إلخ .
وقوله : ( وتمسُّكاً): عطف على (وقوفاً).
قوله: (بظواهر نصوص شرعيَّة) أي : والأخذ بالظواهر أصل من أصول الكفر.
قوله : (سلفهم) : بدل من (أئمتنا)، وقوله فيما يأتي : (وخلفهم) عطف على (سلفهم) ، والمراد بالسَّلف ما قبل الخمس مئة ، ومنهم الأئمة الأربعة .
واشتبه الأمر على أقوام وقوفاً مع الأمور العادية، وتمسكاً بظواهر نصوص شرعية، فقال قوم بالجهة، وقال آخرون بالجسميَّة، ويلزم منهما الحلول والاتصال أو الانفصال، تعالى الله عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً. وأجاب أئمتُنا سَلَفُهم بأنَّ الله تعالى منزّه عن صفات الحوادث، مع تفويض معاني هذه النصوص إليه تعالى إيثاراً للطَّريق الأسلم، وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله ، وخَلَفُهم بتعيين محامل صحيحة إبطالاً لمذهب الضَّالين وإرشاداً للقاصرين، فحملوا اليد على القدرة، والوجه على الذَّات ،
His saying: 'Glorified be He! His signs have indicated the necessity of His existence... etc.': This is the result of what preceded, meaning: Since you have known from what has preceded His description with those attributes, He is glorified, His signs have indicated... etc., and in the speech there is an omitted waw along with what is conjoined to it, meaning: and His transcendence above imperfections. And we only said that in order for the correct arrangement of His saying: 'And the matter became confused for some groups... etc.' to be valid, because it only follows from transcendence above imperfections, so reflect. His saying: 'And the matter became confused for some groups' means: they are the Mu'tazila. His saying: 'Taking a stance according to ordinary matters' is a reason for what precedes; i.e., the matter became confused for them because of their stopping... etc. His saying: 'And holding fast' is conjoined to 'taking a stance'. His saying: 'To the apparent meanings of scriptural texts' means: and adhering to apparent meanings is a root of disbelief. His saying: 'Their predecessors' is a substitute for 'our imams', and his saying later: 'And their later scholars' is conjoined to 'their predecessors', and what is meant by the predecessors is those before 500 AH, among them the four imams. And the matter became confused for some groups, taking a stance with ordinary matters and holding fast to apparent meanings of scriptural texts. So one group said direction, and another said corporeality, and from both follow indwelling and connection or separation. Exalted is God above that with a great exaltation. And our imams, their predecessors, answered that God Most High is transcendent above the attributes of contingents, while consigning the meanings of these texts to Him Most High, preferring the safer path, and none knows its interpretation except God. And their later scholars (answered) by specifying correct interpretations, to invalidate the doctrine of the misguided and guide those of limited capacity. So they interpreted hand as power, and face as essence.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a classification and response to a theological problem. It first notes that the previous discussion on divine attributes leads to the conclusion that God's signs prove His necessary existence and transcendence. The author then introduces a confusion: some groups (the Mu'tazila) became confused because they relied on ordinary appearances and literal interpretations of scriptural texts. This led some to claim God has direction (jiha) and others to claim corporeality (jismiyya), which imply hulul (indwelling) or ittisal/infisal (connection/separation) — all impossible for God. The author then explains the two correct responses: the salaf (scholars before 500 AH, including the four imams) affirmed tanzih and practiced tafwid (consigning the meaning to God), considering it the safer path. The khalaf (later scholars) practiced ta'wil (interpretation), providing specific meanings like 'hand' as power and 'face' as essence, to refute deviant sects and guide those with limited understanding. The commentary clarifies that the confusion only arises from ignoring tanzih, and identifies the groups as Mu'tazila.

اتصال
انفصال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the salaf and khalaf contradict each other because the salaf practice tafwid while the khalaf practice ta'wil.
Correction: Both groups uphold tanzih; they differ only in method. The salaf preferred tafwid as safer, while the khalaf used ta'wil to refute deviance and guide others.

C) Salaf claim God has direction; khalaf claim God has corporeality.
D) Salaf use rational arguments; khalaf use transmitted texts.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila erred by taking literal meanings without affirming tanzih, while the salaf and khalaf correctly upheld God's transcendence through tafwid and ta'wil respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila considered themselves to be faithfully following the texts, yet they fell into error. Are there areas in your life where you hold onto a 'literal' understanding of a religious ruling or text without considering Allah's ultimate wisdom and transcendence? Could that be leading you to a misguided conclusion?
?
The salaf chose the safer path of tafwid because they prioritized avoiding error over intellectual satisfaction. Are you willing to accept theological ambiguity in your faith for the sake of safety, or do you demand a full explanation? What does that say about your trust in Allah's knowledge and your own humility?

Interpreting al-Istiwa' as Dominion and Mercy
Al-Sawi explains al-istiwa' as al-istila' with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi interprets the divine attribute of istiwa' as absolute dominion and mercy, avoiding anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Al-istiwa' means absolute divine control and mercy, not physical sitting.

Al-Sawi tackles one of the most delicate theological issues: the meaning of Allah's istiwa' over the Throne. Drawing on Arabic poetry and mystical aphorisms, he shows that the term signifies complete sovereignty and merciful encompassment. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the evidence he cites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage from Al-Sawi and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow from definition to poetic evidence to aphorism to Quranic support.
The passage is dense but concise.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Notice the poetic line about Bishr gaining control over Iraq, and the aphorism of Ibn 'Ata' Allah. Ask yourself how each supports the interpretation of istiwa' as dominion and mercy.
The poetry shows linguistic usage, the aphorism shows theological application.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Sawi concludes that the Throne is hidden in Allah's mercy, supported by the verse 'My mercy encompasses all things.' Reflect on how this interpretation preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والاستواء على الاستيلاء) أي: لأنَّه أحد معنييه، ومنه قول الشاعر: قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق. وفي آخر حكم ابن عطاء الله السكندري: (يا من استوى برحمانيته على عرشه، فصار العرش غيباً في رحمانيَّته، كما صارت العوالم غيباً في عرشه)، فهو يشير إلى أنَّ معنى الآية: أنَّ العرش وإن كان أكبر المخلوقات وكلُّها مغيبة فيه هو صغير بالنسبة لرحمة الله، ومغيب فيها كما غيبت العوالم فيه. ويؤيده قوله تعالى : (وَرَحْمَتِي وَسِعَتْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ).
His saying: (and al-istiwa' is upon al-istila') i.e., because it is one of its two meanings, and from it is the saying of the poet: "Bishr has become established over Iraq without sword and bloodshed." And at the end of the aphorism of Ibn 'Ata' Allah al-Sakandari: "O He who became established by His mercy upon His throne, so the throne became hidden in His mercy, just as the worlds became hidden in His throne." So he indicates that the meaning of the verse is that the throne, although it is the greatest of created things and all of them are hidden within it, is small relative to the mercy of Allah, and is hidden in it just as the worlds are hidden in it. And it is supported by His saying, Exalted: "And My mercy encompasses all things."
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the term 'al-istiwa' 'ala al-istila'. The author begins by stating that the phrase 'al-istiwa' 'ala al-istila' (being established upon overpowering) is one of the two meanings of 'istiwa''. He then provides linguistic evidence from a line of poetry where 'istawa' is used to mean 'gained control over' (Bishr gaining control over Iraq). He then cites Ibn 'Ata' Allah's aphorism, which interprets the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa' not as a physical sitting but as God's mercy encompassing the throne so completely that the throne becomes hidden within that mercy, just as the entire universe is hidden within the throne. The author concludes that this understanding is supported by the Quranic verse 'And My mercy encompasses all things.' The passage thus presents a metaphorical interpretation of istiwa' as absolute dominion and mercy, avoiding any anthropomorphism.

الاستيلاء
العرش
الرحمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that istiwa' means Allah physically sitting on the Throne.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that istiwa' linguistically means dominion (istila'), and the verse about mercy encompassing all things indicates that it refers to Allah's merciful control, not physical location.

B) Dominion and control

Al-Ghazali's Poetic Defense of Divine Transcendence
Al-Ghazali answers Al-Zamakhshari's query about istiwa' with a poem on divine unknowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ghazali's argument that human ignorance of one's own self and inner processes proves the impossibility of knowing the modality (kayfiyya) of God's attributes like istiwa'.

Core Idea
If you cannot know your own spirit, do not ask how God is established.

This lesson presents a famous exchange between Al-Zamakhshari and Al-Ghazali. Al-Zamakhshari asked about the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa.' Al-Ghazali replied first with a rational point and then with a poem, both driving home the same truth: since we cannot fully comprehend our own selves, we cannot demand to know the modality of God's actions. Read on to see how the poem uses everyday mysteries to reinforce this.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote and rational argument
Start by reading the prose introduction: Al-Zamakhshari's question and Al-Ghazali's initial rational point about not knowing oneself with a 'how' or 'where'.
Notice how Al-Ghazali uses a simple analogy from self-knowledge.
2
Analyze the poem's examples
Read the poem line by line. Identify each example Al-Ghazali gives: the spirit, the breath, the intellect, sleep, digestion. Ask yourself: what do these have in common?
All are aspects of ourselves we cannot fully grasp.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The poem ends with a strong affirmation of divine transcendence: God has no 'where' or 'how', He is above all, and yet He is present. Consider how this resolves the question about istiwa'.
The key is that we affirm the attribute without asking 'how'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل الزمخشري أبا حامد الغزالي عن هذه الآية، فأجابه بقوله : إذا استحال أن تعرف نفسك بكيفيّة أو أينيَّة فكيف يليق بعبوديتك أن تصف الربوبية بأين أو كيف، وهو مقدَّس عن الأين والكيف؟! ثم جعل يقول :

قل لمن يفهم عني ما أقولُ
قصر القول فذا شرح يطولُ
ثم سر غامض من دونه
ضربت والله أعناق الفحول
أنت لا تعرف إياك ولا
تدري من أنت ولا كيف الوصول
لا ولا تدري صفاتٍ ركبت
فيك حارث في خفاياها العقول
أين منكَ الروح في جوهرها
هل تراها فترى كيف تجول
وكذا الأنفاس هل تحصرهـا
لا ولا تدري متى عنك تزول
أين منكَ العقل والفهم وإذا
غلب النوم فقل لي يا جهول
أنت أكل الخبز لا تعرفه
كيف يجري منك أم كيف تبول
فإذا كانت طواياك التي
بين جنبيك كذا فيها ضلول
كيف تدري من على العرش استوى
لا تقل كيف استوى كيف النزول
كيف يحكى الرب أم كيف يرى
فلعمري ليس ذا إلا فضول
فهو لا أين ولا كيف له
وهو رب الكيف والكيف يحول
وهو فوق الفوق لا فوق له
وهو في كل النواحي لا يزول
جل ذاتا وصفات و سما
وتعالى قدره عما نقول
And Al-Zamakhshari asked Abu Hamid al-Ghazali about this verse, and he answered him saying: 'If it is impossible for you to know yourself with a 'how' or a 'where', then how is it befitting for your slavery to attribute 'where' or 'how' to the Lord, and He is sanctified from 'where' and 'how'?!' Then he began to recite:

Say to whoever understands from me what I say:
The speech is brief, so this is an explanation that would be lengthy.
Then a deep secret, beneath which
the necks of the stallions have been broken, by Allah.
You do not know yourself nor
do you know who you are, nor how to reach.
Nor do you know the attributes that are combined
in you, of which the intellects are perplexed in their hidden aspects.
Where is the spirit within its essence?
Do you see it so that you see how it moves?
Likewise the breaths, can you contain them?
No, nor do you know when they depart from you.
Where is the intellect and understanding, and when
sleep overcomes you, say to me, O ignorant one:
You eat bread and do not know
how it proceeds from you, or how you urinate.
If your inner thoughts that are
between your sides are so misguiding,
how can you know who is established on the Throne?
Do not say 'how is He established?' or 'how does He descend?'
How is the Lord described or how is He seen?
By my life, this is nothing but excess.
He has no 'where' and no 'how',
and He is the Lord of 'how' and 'how' changes.
He is above the above, with no above for Him,
and He is in every direction, not disappearing.
Glorified be His Essence and Attributes, exalted be He,
and high be His power above what we say.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents an anecdote in which Al-Zamakhshari asks Al-Ghazali about the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa'. Al-Ghazali's response consists of two parts: a rational argument and a poem. First, he argues that since a person cannot fully know their own self with a 'how' (kayfiyya) or a 'where' (ayniyya), it is inappropriate to ascribe such categories to God, who is sanctified from them. Then he recites a poem that expands this theme. The poem uses a series of examples from human experience: the spirit (al-ruh), the breath, the intellect, sleeping, digestion—all of which operate in ways that are mysterious and not fully grasped by the person. The poem argues that if such basic aspects of one's own existence are unknown, then it is absurd to ask how God (who is established on the Throne) does so. The poem concludes with a strong affirmation of divine transcendence: God has no 'where' or 'how' (even though He is the Creator of such categories), He is above all spatial concepts, and He is present in all directions (as a manifestation of His power) without being limited. The passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position of affirming the attribute of istiwa' while negating any knowledge of the modality (kayfiyya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem denies the attribute of istiwa' entirely.
Correction: The poem denies knowing the modality (kayfiyya) of istiwa', not the attribute itself. Al-Ghazali affirms that God is established on the Throne, but we cannot ask 'how'.

B) Human ignorance of oneself proves the impossibility of knowing God's modality
C) The throne is literally Allah's seat
D) The soul is similar to God

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali teaches that we must affirm God's attributes like istiwa' without asking 'how', just as we accept our own mysteries.

The poem says your inner thoughts are 'misguiding'. How often do you trust your own reasoning to judge what God 'should' be like? Describe one way your intellect has led you to question divine wisdom. Will you now surrender that assumption?
?
Al-Ghazali calls it 'excess' to ask 'how does the Lord descend?' Yet you have probably asked such questions silently. What does this lesson reveal about your own arrogance? Write down one specific question about God you will replace with awe and silence.

Defending Ghazali: Three Answers on Divine Power
Objection to Ghazali's statement and three answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct ways scholars defended al-Ghazali's statement that nothing more perfect than what exists is possible, reconciling divine power with the order of creation.

Core Idea
Ghazali's paradox defends both God's power and the perfection of His creation.

Al-Ghazali once made a striking claim: there is nothing more perfect than the world as it is. This raised an obvious objection—does this limit God's power? In this passage, the commentator presents three sophisticated answers that reinterpret the statement to protect divine omnipotence while affirming the wisdom of creation. Let's explore each answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the commentator first quotes Ghazali's statement and then reports the objection that it suggests inability in God.
The objection is that the statement 'لا أبدع مما كان' might imply God cannot create something better.
2
Understand the first answer: possibility refers to creatures
The first answer reinterprets 'imkan' as the possibility of creatures. The meaning is that creatures cannot change what God has created, so God's power is not limited.
This answer negates creatures' power, not God's.
3
Understand the second answer: possibility refers to God's knowledge
The second answer shifts focus to God's eternal knowledge. Since God's knowledge eternally determined this world, no other world could be created without contradicting that knowledge. The Quranic verse is explained as rational possibility apart from actual decree.
This preserves divine omniscience and power.
4
Understand the third answer: temporal cannot become eternal
The third answer states that the statement means it is impossible to make a temporal thing eternal, as that is logically impossible. This does not limit God's power because logical impossibilities are not within power.
This answer focuses on the nature of things.
5
Reflect on how these answers safeguard divine power
Consider how each answer reinterprets 'imkan' to avoid attributing any limitation to God. The passage shows that Ghazali's statement, when properly understood, affirms God's perfection.
The answers together show the depth of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذا قال بعض أهل العرفان لما شاهد من عجيب الإتقان : ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان. قوله : (بعض أهل العرفان هو حجة الإسلام الغزالي) واستشكل قوله قديماً بأنَّه يوهم العجز، وهو عليه محال، تعالى الله عنه. وأجيب عنه بأجوبة؛ منها : أنَّ المراد بالإمكان إمكان الخلائق فالمعنى : ليس في إمكان الخلائق تغيير ما أراده الله وأبدعه، فالمنفي تعلق قدرة الخلق. ومنها : أنَّ المراد إمكان الله باعتبار تعلق علمه أزلاً بإيجاد هذا العالم على هذا النظام، وتعلق القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على طبق ما سبق به العلم، وإلَّا لانقلب العلم جهلاً، فليس من الممكن إيجاد عالم غير هذا الموجود، وأما قوله تعالى: {إِنَّا لَقَدِرُونَ عَلَى أَن نُّبَدِّلَ خَيْرًا مِّنْهُمْ} فباعتبار الجواز العقلي بقطع النظر عن تعلّق العلم. تعلّق. ومنها : أنَّ المراد ليس في الإمكان جعل الحادث قديماً؛ لعدم القدرة بذلك؛ لأنَّ الشَّيء إمَّا قديم أو حادث، فالحادث يستحيل خروجه.
Thus some people of gnosis said when they witnessed the wonders of perfection: 'There is nothing more perfect than what has been in existence.' He said: '(Some people of gnosis) is the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali.' And his statement was objected to in the past because it suggests inability, while that is impossible for God, exalted is God above it. And it was answered with answers: among them: that what is meant by possibility is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is: it is not within the possibility of creatures to change what God willed and created, so what is denied is the attachment of creatures' power. And among them: that what is meant is God's possibility in terms of His eternal knowledge attaching to bringing this world into existence upon this system, and the executive power attaches only in accordance with what preceded in knowledge, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance, so it is not possible to bring into existence a world other than this existing one. And as for His saying, 'Indeed, We are able to replace them with better ones,' it is in terms of rational possibility disregarding the attachment of knowledge. Attached. And among them: that what is meant is that it is not possible to make the temporal eternal, because there is no power for that, because a thing is either eternal or temporal, so the temporal cannot escape it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer sequence concerning a famous statement by Ghazali: 'There is nothing more perfect than what has been in existence.' The commentator first identifies the source of the statement, then reports an objection that it suggests God is unable to create something better, which is impossible for God. Three answers are given. First answer: 'possibility' refers to the creatures' capacity—they cannot alter God's creation. Second answer: 'possibility' refers to God's own possibility in relation to His eternal knowledge; since His knowledge determines what will exist, no other world could have been created without contradicting His knowledge. The Quranic verse about replacing people with better ones is interpreted as referring to rational possibility apart from God's actual decree. Third answer: 'possibility' concerns the impossibility of turning a temporal thing into an eternal thing, as that is logically impossible. The passage defends Ghazali's statement without diminishing God's power.

الجواز العقلي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's statement denies God's ability to create a better world, limiting His power.
Correction: The passage shows that the statement is about creatures' power, God's eternal knowledge, or logical impossibilities, all of which affirm God's absolute power.

Checkpoint
What is the second answer's interpretation of 'imkan' (possibility) in Ghazali's statement?
A) It refers to the possibility of creatures changing God's creation.
B) It refers to God's possibility in relation to His eternal knowledge, so no other world is possible.
C) It refers to the rational possibility of creating a better world.
D) It refers to the impossibility of turning the eternal into temporal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's statement is defended by reinterpreting 'possibility' in three ways: as creatures' power, as God's knowledge, and as logical impossibility, all preserving divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second answer says God's knowledge necessitates this world. If you truly believed that every detail of your existence was known and willed by God from eternity, how would that change your response to hardships? What specific habit would you need to change to live out this belief?
?
The third answer shows that making a temporal thing eternal is impossible. Reflect on something you are holding onto that is temporary, as if it were eternal. What would it cost you to release it and accept its temporality as part of God's perfect order?

The Thirteen Impossible Attributes for God
Al-Sawi enumerates the thirteen impossible attributes for God and explains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thirteen attributes that are impossible for God according to al-Sawi and the logical basis for their impossibility.

Core Idea
Necessary existence admits no negation; thus these thirteen attributes must be impossible.

After establishing that certain attributes are necessary for God, al-Sawi turns to the opposite: what can never be true of Him. Because the Necessary admits no negation, these thirteen impossibilities follow directly. This passage lists them and briefly explains each, laying the groundwork for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with its translation
First read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full list and flow. Notice the first two: non-existence and temporality, then the rest.
The passage starts with a reminder that the necessary does not admit negation.
2
Identify the thirteen items
The passage lists thirteen impossibilities. Count them as you read: non-existence, temporality, paths of non-existence, similarity to contingents, lack of self-subsistence, lack of unicity, ignorance, death, and then the explanations for each. The exact list can be extracted from the text.
Some items like 'similarity to contingents' include multiple aspects (substance, accident, etc.)
3
Understand the logical basis
Notice the underlying principle: what is necessary cannot be negated. Each impossible attribute is the negation of a necessary attribute. The passage uses concepts of contradiction and opposition (contradictory, privation, etc.) to explain the relationships.
For example, non-existence is the contradictory of existence, and temporality is existence after non-existence.
4
Reflect on the nuance about counting
The passage notes that if one counts attributive attributes, the number of impossibilities becomes twenty. This shows how different theological frameworks can expand or contract the list.

Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the thirteen attributes that are impossible for God, as presented by al-Sawi in his commentary on al-Kharidat. The author begins by reiterating the principle: since necessary existence cannot admit negation, certain attributes are impossible. He lists the first two: non-existence (العدم) and temporality (الحدوث). He then discusses a proof related to impossibility of accepting opposites, noting that the commentator omitted a conditional form and clarified a hidden implication. The passage then lists more impossibilities: paths of non-existence (i.e., annihilation), similarity to contingents in various ways (substance, accident, etc.), lack of self-subsistence, lack of unicity, ignorance (both complex and simple, including doubt, heedlessness, forgetfulness, sleep, distraction), and death. After listing, the hashya explains each term: non-existence is equivalent to the contradictory of existence, with nuance depending on the view on states (aḥwāl). Temporality is existence after non-existence. Paths of non-existence is equivalent to contradictory of permanence. Similarity to contingents is contradictory of dissimilarity. Lack of self-subsistence is contradictory of self-subsistence. Lack of unicity is contradictory of unicity. Ignorance is opposed to knowledge: complex ignorance by opposition of opposites, simple ignorance by privation and possession. Death is opposed to life by privation and possession. The passage ends mid-explanation of death.

Nonexistence and Resemblance: Negating Imperfections from Allah
Sanusi lists the impossibilities of nonexistence and resemblance to contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be characterized by nonexistence (fanā') or resemblance to contingent beings, and how these negations affirm His perfection.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond annihilation and cannot be compared to any created thing.

Sanusi begins the enumeration of impossible attributes by stating that Allah cannot be subject to nonexistence (annihilation) and cannot resemble contingent beings in any way. These are foundational negations that protect the concept of God from limitations inherent in creation. This lesson examines these two impossibilities and the rational basis for denying them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on nonexistence and resemblance
Read the Arabic text and translation that define these two impossible attributes. Notice how the commentator clarifies each term.
The matn lists many aspects of resemblance: corporeality, accidentality, indwelling, etc.
2
Analyze the logical negations
Consider why nonexistence is impossible: it would imply that Allah is not eternal. Resemblance is impossible because it would make Him like His creatures, undermining His uniqueness.
3
Connect to the broader theology
Understand that these negations are the basis for affirmations of Allah's everlastingness (baqā') and incomparability (mukhālafa).
This relates to the necessary attributes taught earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطرقُ العدم، ويسمى الفناء. - والمماثلة للحوادث؛ من جرميَّة أو عرضيَّة، أو حلول، أو اتصال أو انفصال، أو بعد أو قرب أو كبر أو صِغَر . - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى عدم القيام بنفسه؛ بأن يفتقر إلى محلّ أو مخصص . - وعدم الوحدانية، بأن يكون ذا كثرة في ذاته أو صفاته، أو يكون له شريك في فعل من الأفعال. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الجهل، مركباً أو بسيطاً، أو ما معناه : من ظنّ أو غفلة أو نسيان أو نوم، أو اشتغال بشأن. ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموتُ عن القدم، إذ نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو يصدق بالوجود بعد العدم الَّذي هو الحدوث بالعدم المنقطع بالوجود واللاحق . قوله : ( وطرقُ العدم): هو مساو لنقيض البقاء. قوله : (والمماثلة للحوادث هي مساوية لنقيض المخالفة. قوله : (عدم القيام بنفسه : هو نقيض القيام، وكذا عدم الوحدانية نقيض الوحدانية . قوله: (الجهل مركباً أو بسيطاً مقابلة العلم للأوَّل من مقابلة الضّدَّين، وللثّاني : من مقابلة العدم للملكة. قوله : (الموت) : مقابلته للحياة من تقابل العدم، والملكة إن قلنا : إنَّ المستحيلات والعجزُ، وما في معناه : من فتور أو نصب. - والكراهية : أي : عدم الإرادة بأن يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، أو تصدر الكائنات عنه تعالى بالتَّعليل أو بالطبع، لما يلزم من قِدَم العالم، الذي قام البرهان القاطع على حدوثه؛ وورد الشَّرع به؛ لأنَّه يجب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، والقائل بذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين، كما تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل بالعلة والفاعل بالطَّبع : من أنَّ العلة لا تتوقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع، والطَّبيعة تتوقف على ذلك. ومما يدلُّ على بطلانها اختلاف أنواع العالم على كثرتها، إذ معلولُ العِلَّة والطبيعة لا يختلف. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى البكم؛ أي: عدم الكلام بوجود آفة تمنع منه، وفي معناه السكوت النَّفسي . الموت عدم الحياة، وتقابل الضّدّين إن قلنا : إنَّه أمر وجودي. قوله : (والعجز ) : هو مساو لنقيض القدرة. قوله : (والكراهيَّة) هي مساوية لنقيض الإرادة. : قوله : (البكم ) : هو وما بعده من الصَّمم والعمى، إما من مقابلة الضّدّين، أو العدم والملكة . قوله : (الشكوت النَّفسي) : أي : وأمَّا السُّكوت اللفظيُّ؛ فلا يتوهم في حق الله ؛ لاستحالة الكلام اللفظي عليه تعالى .
And the ways of nonexistence, and it is called annihilation. And resemblance to contingent beings: whether corporeality or accidentality, or indwelling, or connection or separation, or distance or proximity, or bigness or smallness. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, to not be self-subsistent; such that He would need a place or a particularizer. And not being unique, such that He would have multiplicity in His essence or His attributes, or have a partner in any action. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, ignorance, whether compound or simple, or whatever implies it: like doubt or heedlessness or forgetfulness or sleep, or being occupied with some matter. And death is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, because it negates pre-eternity, for the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity, and it applies to existence after nonexistence which is origination by nonexistence cut off by existence and subsequent. His saying: 'And the ways of nonexistence': it is equivalent to the opposite of everlastingness. His saying: 'And resemblance to contingent beings' is equivalent to the opposite of dissimilarity. His saying: 'Not being self-subsistent': it is the opposite of self-subsistence, and likewise not being unique is the opposite of uniqueness. His saying: 'Ignorance, compound or simple': its opposition to knowledge for the first is a contradiction of opposites, and for the second is the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: 'Death': its opposition to life is the opposition of privation and possession if we say that death is nonexistence of life, and contradiction of opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity, and what is in its meaning: like languor or weariness. And dislike: that is, absence of will such that what He does not will occurs in His dominion, or that beings come from Him, may He be exalted, by causation or by nature, because it necessitates the pre-eternity of the world, against which decisive proof has been established for its temporal origination; and the Revealed Law came with it; because the cause must be concomitant with its effect and the natural property with its imprint, and the one who says that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims, as has preceded, and the difference between the agent by causation and the agent by nature has preceded: that the cause does not depend on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an impediment, while the nature depends on that. And among what indicates its falsity is the diversity of the types of the world despite their abundance, for the effect of the cause and the nature does not differ. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, muteness: that is, absence of speech due to a defect that prevents it, and in its meaning is internal silence. Death is non-existence of life, and it is the opposition of two opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity': it is equivalent to the opposite of power. His saying: 'And dislike': it is equivalent to the opposite of will. His saying: 'Muteness': it and what follows of deafness and blindness are either from the opposition of opposites, or privation and possession. His saying: 'Internal silence': that is, as for verbal silence, it is not imagined of God, because verbal speech is impossible for Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the impossible attributes (al-mustahilat) of Allah, drawn from the Kharida text with commentary by al-Sawi. The author first enumerates the attributes that cannot be attributed to God, each contrasted with a necessary attribute.

The list begins with 'ways of nonexistence' (turūq al-ʿadam), also called annihilation (fanā'), which is the opposite of everlastingness (baqā'). Next is resemblance to contingent beings (al-mumāthala lil-ḥawādith), including corporeality, accidentality, indwelling, connection, separation, distance, proximity, bigness, smallness — these are all negated because God is unlike any created thing. Then, not being self-subsistent (ʿadam al-qiyām bi-nafsih) is impossible; this would mean needing a place or a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). Also impossible is lack of oneness (ʿadam al-waḥdāniyya): having multiplicity in essence or attributes, or having a partner in any act.

Ignorance (jahl) is impossible, whether compound or simple, and anything implying it like doubt, heedlessness, forgetfulness, sleep, or occupation with affairs. Death (mawt) is impossible because it negates pre-eternity (qidam); the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity (lā qidam), which would imply origination after nonexistence.

The commentary then explains each term: 'ways of nonexistence' equals opposite of everlastingness; 'resemblance' equals opposite of dissimilarity; 'not self-subsistent' equals opposite of self-subsistence; 'not unique' equals opposite of uniqueness. Ignorance is opposed to knowledge: for compound ignorance, it is contradiction of opposites (taqābul al-ḍiddayn); for simple ignorance, it is privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka). Death is opposed to life as privation and possession if death is non-existence of life, but as contradiction of opposites if death is considered an existential thing.

The next section discusses incapacity (ʿajz) and its meanings like languor or weariness, and dislike (karāhiyya) which is absence of will — that something occurs in His dominion against His will, or that beings originate from Him by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that such views imply the pre-eternity of the world, which is refuted by decisive proof and revelation. The agent by causation (al-fāʿil bil-ʿilla) and agent by nature (al-fāʿil bi-ṭ-ṭabʿ) are distinguished: the cause does not depend on conditions or absence of impediments, while nature does. The diversity of the world's types shows their falsity because effects of a single cause or nature do not differ.

Finally, muteness (bakam) is impossible: absence of speech due to a defect. This includes internal silence (sukūt nafsī). Verbal silence is not imagined of God because verbal speech is impossible for Him. The commentary adds that muteness, deafness, blindness are opposed either as contradictions of opposites or as privation and possession.

Key Terms
طرق العدم
الفناء
المماثلة للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying attributes like becoming non-existent or having a body implies deficiency in God.
Correction: These negations actually affirm God's perfection by clarifying what He must be free from; they are not limitations but protections of His transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'ways of nonexistence' (طرق العدم) refer to?
A) The path to non-existence
B) Annihilation (الفناء)
C) Resemblance
D) Ignorance

Key Takeaway
Allah is free from non-existence and any resemblance to His creation; these negations establish His majesty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's power, do you ever imagine it as a big, human-like power? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that Allah bears no resemblance to anything you have ever seen or experienced?
?
If you truly believed that Allah cannot cease to exist and is beyond all comparison, would you still worry about your own mortality and weakness in the same way? What would it take for that belief to change your daily fears?
?
In moments of difficulty, do you ever expect Allah to act in a way that resembles human intervention? How can you train yourself to trust in His absolute transcendence while still seeking His help?
