Self-Subsistence and Oneness: Essential Divine Perfections
Sanusi enumerates the impossibilities of not being self-subsistent and lacking oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah must be self-subsistent and one, and why lacking these would be impossible.

Core Idea
Allah depends on nothing and has no partner or multiplicity.

After negating annihilation and resemblance, Sanusi affirms that Allah cannot be dependent on anything (needing a place or particularizer) nor can He have multiplicity in essence, attributes, or have a partner. This lesson explains the rational necessity of these negations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on self-subsistence and oneness
Read the Arabic text and translation that state the impossibilities of not being self-subsistent and lacking oneness.
The passage explicitly mentions needing a place or particularizer as a sign of dependence.
2
Reflect on the implications of dependence
If Allah were not self-subsistent, He would be like created beings that depend on external factors. Similarly, lack of oneness would mean multiple sources of divinity, which violates tawhid.
The negation of multiplicity includes both essence, attributes, and actions.
3
Connect these to the necessary attributes
These negations are the inverse of Allah's necessary attributes of self-subsistence (qiyām bi-nafsih) and oneness (waḥdāniyya).
Understanding these helps strengthen belief in tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطرقُ العدم، ويسمى الفناء. - والمماثلة للحوادث؛ من جرميَّة أو عرضيَّة، أو حلول، أو اتصال أو انفصال، أو بعد أو قرب أو كبر أو صِغَر . - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى عدم القيام بنفسه؛ بأن يفتقر إلى محلّ أو مخصص . - وعدم الوحدانية، بأن يكون ذا كثرة في ذاته أو صفاته، أو يكون له شريك في فعل من الأفعال. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الجهل، مركباً أو بسيطاً، أو ما معناه : من ظنّ أو غفلة أو نسيان أو نوم، أو اشتغال بشأن. ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموتُ عن القدم، إذ نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو يصدق بالوجود بعد العدم الَّذي هو الحدوث بالعدم المنقطع بالوجود واللاحق . قوله : ( وطرقُ العدم): هو مساو لنقيض البقاء. قوله : (والمماثلة للحوادث هي مساوية لنقيض المخالفة. قوله : (عدم القيام بنفسه : هو نقيض القيام، وكذا عدم الوحدانية نقيض الوحدانية . قوله: (الجهل مركباً أو بسيطاً مقابلة العلم للأوَّل من مقابلة الضّدَّين، وللثّاني : من مقابلة العدم للملكة. قوله : (الموت) : مقابلته للحياة من تقابل العدم، والملكة إن قلنا : إنَّ المستحيلات والعجزُ، وما في معناه : من فتور أو نصب. - والكراهية : أي : عدم الإرادة بأن يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، أو تصدر الكائنات عنه تعالى بالتَّعليل أو بالطبع، لما يلزم من قِدَم العالم، الذي قام البرهان القاطع على حدوثه؛ وورد الشَّرع به؛ لأنَّه يجب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، والقائل بذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين، كما تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل بالعلة والفاعل بالطَّبع : من أنَّ العلة لا تتوقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع، والطَّبيعة تتوقف على ذلك. ومما يدلُّ على بطلانها اختلاف أنواع العالم على كثرتها، إذ معلولُ العِلَّة والطبيعة لا يختلف. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى البكم؛ أي: عدم الكلام بوجود آفة تمنع منه، وفي معناه السكوت النَّفسي . الموت عدم الحياة، وتقابل الضّدّين إن قلنا : إنَّه أمر وجودي. قوله : (والعجز ) : هو مساو لنقيض القدرة. قوله : (والكراهيَّة) هي مساوية لنقيض الإرادة. : قوله : (البكم ) : هو وما بعده من الصَّمم والعمى، إما من مقابلة الضّدّين، أو العدم والملكة . قوله : (الشكوت النَّفسي) : أي : وأمَّا السُّكوت اللفظيُّ؛ فلا يتوهم في حق الله ؛ لاستحالة الكلام اللفظي عليه تعالى .
And the ways of nonexistence, and it is called annihilation. And resemblance to contingent beings: whether corporeality or accidentality, or indwelling, or connection or separation, or distance or proximity, or bigness or smallness. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, to not be self-subsistent; such that He would need a place or a particularizer. And not being unique, such that He would have multiplicity in His essence or His attributes, or have a partner in any action. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, ignorance, whether compound or simple, or whatever implies it: like doubt or heedlessness or forgetfulness or sleep, or being occupied with some matter. And death is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, because it negates pre-eternity, for the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity, and it applies to existence after nonexistence which is origination by nonexistence cut off by existence and subsequent. His saying: 'And the ways of nonexistence': it is equivalent to the opposite of everlastingness. His saying: 'And resemblance to contingent beings' is equivalent to the opposite of dissimilarity. His saying: 'Not being self-subsistent': it is the opposite of self-subsistence, and likewise not being unique is the opposite of uniqueness. His saying: 'Ignorance, compound or simple': its opposition to knowledge for the first is a contradiction of opposites, and for the second is the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: 'Death': its opposition to life is the opposition of privation and possession if we say that death is nonexistence of life, and contradiction of opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity, and what is in its meaning: like languor or weariness. And dislike: that is, absence of will such that what He does not will occurs in His dominion, or that beings come from Him, may He be exalted, by causation or by nature, because it necessitates the pre-eternity of the world, against which decisive proof has been established for its temporal origination; and the Revealed Law came with it; because the cause must be concomitant with its effect and the natural property with its imprint, and the one who says that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims, as has preceded, and the difference between the agent by causation and the agent by nature has preceded: that the cause does not depend on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an impediment, while the nature depends on that. And among what indicates its falsity is the diversity of the types of the world despite their abundance, for the effect of the cause and the nature does not differ. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, muteness: that is, absence of speech due to a defect that prevents it, and in its meaning is internal silence. Death is non-existence of life, and it is the opposition of two opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity': it is equivalent to the opposite of power. His saying: 'And dislike': it is equivalent to the opposite of will. His saying: 'Muteness': it and what follows of deafness and blindness are either from the opposition of opposites, or privation and possession. His saying: 'Internal silence': that is, as for verbal silence, it is not imagined of God, because verbal speech is impossible for Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the impossible attributes (al-mustahilat) of Allah, drawn from the Kharida text with commentary by al-Sawi. The author first enumerates the attributes that cannot be attributed to God, each contrasted with a necessary attribute.

The list begins with 'ways of nonexistence' (turūq al-ʿadam), also called annihilation (fanā'), which is the opposite of everlastingness (baqā'). Next is resemblance to contingent beings (al-mumāthala lil-ḥawādith), including corporeality, accidentality, indwelling, connection, separation, distance, proximity, bigness, smallness — these are all negated because God is unlike any created thing. Then, not being self-subsistent (ʿadam al-qiyām bi-nafsih) is impossible; this would mean needing a place or a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). Also impossible is lack of oneness (ʿadam al-waḥdāniyya): having multiplicity in essence or attributes, or having a partner in any act.

Ignorance (jahl) is impossible, whether compound or simple, and anything implying it like doubt, heedlessness, forgetfulness, sleep, or occupation with affairs. Death (mawt) is impossible because it negates pre-eternity (qidam); the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity (lā qidam), which would imply origination after nonexistence.

The commentary then explains each term: 'ways of nonexistence' equals opposite of everlastingness; 'resemblance' equals opposite of dissimilarity; 'not self-subsistent' equals opposite of self-subsistence; 'not unique' equals opposite of uniqueness. Ignorance is opposed to knowledge: for compound ignorance, it is contradiction of opposites (taqābul al-ḍiddayn); for simple ignorance, it is privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka). Death is opposed to life as privation and possession if death is non-existence of life, but as contradiction of opposites if death is considered an existential thing.

The next section discusses incapacity (ʿajz) and its meanings like languor or weariness, and dislike (karāhiyya) which is absence of will — that something occurs in His dominion against His will, or that beings originate from Him by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that such views imply the pre-eternity of the world, which is refuted by decisive proof and revelation. The agent by causation (al-fāʿil bil-ʿilla) and agent by nature (al-fāʿil bi-ṭ-ṭabʿ) are distinguished: the cause does not depend on conditions or absence of impediments, while nature does. The diversity of the world's types shows their falsity because effects of a single cause or nature do not differ.

Finally, muteness (bakam) is impossible: absence of speech due to a defect. This includes internal silence (sukūt nafsī). Verbal silence is not imagined of God because verbal speech is impossible for Him. The commentary adds that muteness, deafness, blindness are opposed either as contradictions of opposites or as privation and possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'self-subsistent' implies God is a substance or exists in a location.
Correction: Self-subsistence means God exists without needing any support or substrate, not that He is a physical entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'not being self-subsistent' entail?
A) Needing a place or particularizer
B) Having a body
C) Being ignorant
D) Being mortal

Key Takeaway
Allah is self-subsistent and one—He depends on nothing and has no partner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is self-sufficient and depends on nothing, why do you often seek security in people, wealth, or status rather than in Him alone? What step can you take today to shift your trust entirely to Him?
?
If Allah is one and unique, why do you sometimes give 'partners' to Him in your heart—by fearing criticism more than His displeasure, or by loving someone's approval more than His? How will you actively purify your intention this week?
?
When you feel that your existence or worth depends on external factors, how does the truth that Allah is self-subsistent (al-Qayyum) challenge that feeling? What specific thought or action can you use to remind yourself of His independence and your dependence on Him?

Ignorance: The Impossibility of Imperfect Knowledge
Sanusi declares the impossibility of ignorance in all forms for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ignorance in any form is impossible for Allah and how it affirms His perfect knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah knows all things perfectly and is free from any form of ignorance.

Sanusi then addresses ignorance (jahl) and its implications. Ignorance, whether compound or simple, or any state implying it such as doubt, forgetfulness, or sleep, cannot be attributed to Allah. This lesson explains the rational basis for negating ignorance and how this reinforces Allah's attribute of omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ignorance
Read the Arabic text and translation that list the forms of ignorance and their opposites.
The commentator distinguishes between compound ignorance (ignorance of something while being unaware of one's ignorance) and simple ignorance (lack of knowledge).
2
Analyze the types of ignorance
Consider why each type—compound, simple, doubt, forgetfulness, sleep—is impossible for Allah. Each implies a deficiency in knowledge.
Sleep and forgetfulness are considered aspects of imperfection.
3
Relate to Allah's attribute of knowledge
Negating ignorance affirms that Allah's knowledge is perfect, encompassing all things fully.
This is a necessary attribute of Allah.

D) Learning

Key Takeaway
Allah is perfectly knowledgeable; no form of ignorance—compound, simple, or any related deficiency—can be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah is free from all forms of ignorance, including forgetfulness and heedlessness. But you often forget His commands and act as if He is not watching. What specific habit will you change today to live with the awareness that He knows every detail of your life?
?
Compound ignorance is not knowing and thinking you know. Where in your own understanding of Allah might you be guilty of this? What step will you take to replace false certainty with humble, authentic knowledge?
?
This lesson teaches that even simple ignorance is a privation unworthy of Allah. Yet you are content with ignorance about your own purpose. What will you do this week to replace one area of spiritual ignorance with the knowledge that brings you closer to Him?

Death: Negating Non-Existence After Life
Sanusi declares the impossibility of death for Allah with a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why death is impossible for Allah and how it relates to His pre-eternity and everlastingness.

Core Idea
Allah is eternally living and cannot be subject to death.

Sanusi addresses the impossibility of death (mawt) for Allah. Death would imply a beginning to existence after non-existence, which contradicts His pre-eternity. This lesson explains the logical argument against death and how it reinforces Allah's attribute of life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on death
Read the Arabic text and translation that state the impossibility of death and the reasoning behind it.
The passage explains that death negates pre-eternity and implies origination.
2
Understand the argument
Death is the opposite of life. If Allah could die, He would have a beginning, which is impossible for the Eternal.
The commentator discusses whether death is a privation or an existential thing.
3
Connect to Allah's attribute of life
Negating death affirms that Allah is eternally alive (al-Ḥayy).
This is a necessary attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطرقُ العدم، ويسمى الفناء. - والمماثلة للحوادث؛ من جرميَّة أو عرضيَّة، أو حلول، أو اتصال أو انفصال، أو بعد أو قرب أو كبر أو صِغَر . - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى عدم القيام بنفسه؛ بأن يفتقر إلى محلّ أو مخصص . - وعدم الوحدانية، بأن يكون ذا كثرة في ذاته أو صفاته، أو يكون له شريك في فعل من الأفعال. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الجهل، مركباً أو بسيطاً، أو ما معناه : من ظنّ أو غفلة أو نسيان أو نوم، أو اشتغال بشأن. ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموتُ عن القدم، إذ نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو يصدق بالوجود بعد العدم الَّذي هو الحدوث بالعدم المنقطع بالوجود واللاحق . قوله : ( وطرقُ العدم): هو مساو لنقيض البقاء. قوله : (والمماثلة للحوادث هي مساوية لنقيض المخالفة. قوله : (عدم القيام بنفسه : هو نقيض القيام، وكذا عدم الوحدانية نقيض الوحدانية . قوله: (الجهل مركباً أو بسيطاً مقابلة العلم للأوَّل من مقابلة الضّدَّين، وللثّاني : من مقابلة العدم للملكة. قوله : (الموت) : مقابلته للحياة من تقابل العدم، والملكة إن قلنا : إنَّ المستحيلات والعجزُ، وما في معناه : من فتور أو نصب. - والكراهية : أي : عدم الإرادة بأن يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، أو تصدر الكائنات عنه تعالى بالتَّعليل أو بالطبع، لما يلزم من قِدَم العالم، الذي قام البرهان القاطع على حدوثه؛ وورد الشَّرع به؛ لأنَّه يجب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، والقائل بذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين، كما تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل بالعلة والفاعل بالطَّبع : من أنَّ العلة لا تتوقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع، والطَّبيعة تتوقف على ذلك. ومما يدلُّ على بطلانها اختلاف أنواع العالم على كثرتها، إذ معلولُ العِلَّة والطبيعة لا يختلف. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى البكم؛ أي: عدم الكلام بوجود آفة تمنع منه، وفي معناه السكوت النَّفسي . الموت عدم الحياة، وتقابل الضّدّين إن قلنا : إنَّه أمر وجودي. قوله : (والعجز ) : هو مساو لنقيض القدرة. قوله : (والكراهيَّة) هي مساوية لنقيض الإرادة. : قوله : (البكم ) : هو وما بعده من الصَّمم والعمى، إما من مقابلة الضّدّين، أو العدم والملكة . قوله : (الشكوت النَّفسي) : أي : وأمَّا السُّكوت اللفظيُّ؛ فلا يتوهم في حق الله ؛ لاستحالة الكلام اللفظي عليه تعالى .
And the ways of nonexistence, and it is called annihilation. And resemblance to contingent beings: whether corporeality or accidentality, or indwelling, or connection or separation, or distance or proximity, or bigness or smallness. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, to not be self-subsistent; such that He would need a place or a particularizer. And not being unique, such that He would have multiplicity in His essence or His attributes, or have a partner in any action. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, ignorance, whether compound or simple, or whatever implies it: like doubt or heedlessness or forgetfulness or sleep, or being occupied with some matter. And death is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, because it negates pre-eternity, for the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity, and it applies to existence after nonexistence which is origination by nonexistence cut off by existence and subsequent. His saying: 'And the ways of nonexistence': it is equivalent to the opposite of everlastingness. His saying: 'And resemblance to contingent beings' is equivalent to the opposite of dissimilarity. His saying: 'Not being self-subsistent': it is the opposite of self-subsistence, and likewise not being unique is the opposite of uniqueness. His saying: 'Ignorance, compound or simple': its opposition to knowledge for the first is a contradiction of opposites, and for the second is the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: 'Death': its opposition to life is the opposition of privation and possession if we say that death is nonexistence of life, and contradiction of opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity, and what is in its meaning: like languor or weariness. And dislike: that is, absence of will such that what He does not will occurs in His dominion, or that beings come from Him, may He be exalted, by causation or by nature, because it necessitates the pre-eternity of the world, against which decisive proof has been established for its temporal origination; and the Revealed Law came with it; because the cause must be concomitant with its effect and the natural property with its imprint, and the one who says that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims, as has preceded, and the difference between the agent by causation and the agent by nature has preceded: that the cause does not depend on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an impediment, while the nature depends on that. And among what indicates its falsity is the diversity of the types of the world despite their abundance, for the effect of the cause and the nature does not differ. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, muteness: that is, absence of speech due to a defect that prevents it, and in its meaning is internal silence. Death is non-existence of life, and it is the opposition of two opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity': it is equivalent to the opposite of power. His saying: 'And dislike': it is equivalent to the opposite of will. His saying: 'Muteness': it and what follows of deafness and blindness are either from the opposition of opposites, or privation and possession. His saying: 'Internal silence': that is, as for verbal silence, it is not imagined of God, because verbal speech is impossible for Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the impossible attributes (al-mustahilat) of Allah, drawn from the Kharida text with commentary by al-Sawi. The author first enumerates the attributes that cannot be attributed to God, each contrasted with a necessary attribute.

The list begins with 'ways of nonexistence' (turūq al-ʿadam), also called annihilation (fanā'), which is the opposite of everlastingness (baqā'). Next is resemblance to contingent beings (al-mumāthala lil-ḥawādith), including corporeality, accidentality, indwelling, connection, separation, distance, proximity, bigness, smallness — these are all negated because God is unlike any created thing. Then, not being self-subsistent (ʿadam al-qiyām bi-nafsih) is impossible; this would mean needing a place or a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). Also impossible is lack of oneness (ʿadam al-waḥdāniyya): having multiplicity in essence or attributes, or having a partner in any act.

Ignorance (jahl) is impossible, whether compound or simple, and anything implying it like doubt, heedlessness, forgetfulness, sleep, or occupation with affairs. Death (mawt) is impossible because it negates pre-eternity (qidam); the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity (lā qidam), which would imply origination after nonexistence.

The commentary then explains each term: 'ways of nonexistence' equals opposite of everlastingness; 'resemblance' equals opposite of dissimilarity; 'not self-subsistent' equals opposite of self-subsistence; 'not unique' equals opposite of uniqueness. Ignorance is opposed to knowledge: for compound ignorance, it is contradiction of opposites (taqābul al-ḍiddayn); for simple ignorance, it is privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka). Death is opposed to life as privation and possession if death is non-existence of life, but as contradiction of opposites if death is considered an existential thing.

The next section discusses incapacity (ʿajz) and its meanings like languor or weariness, and dislike (karāhiyya) which is absence of will — that something occurs in His dominion against His will, or that beings originate from Him by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that such views imply the pre-eternity of the world, which is refuted by decisive proof and revelation. The agent by causation (al-fāʿil bil-ʿilla) and agent by nature (al-fāʿil bi-ṭ-ṭabʿ) are distinguished: the cause does not depend on conditions or absence of impediments, while nature does. The diversity of the world's types shows their falsity because effects of a single cause or nature do not differ.

Finally, muteness (bakam) is impossible: absence of speech due to a defect. This includes internal silence (sukūt nafsī). Verbal silence is not imagined of God because verbal speech is impossible for Him. The commentary adds that muteness, deafness, blindness are opposed either as contradictions of opposites or as privation and possession.

Key Terms
الموت
القدم
الحدوث
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that death is a natural end for all beings, including God.
Correction: God is eternal living, and death would imply a beginning, which is impossible for the Pre-Eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is death impossible for Allah?
A) Because it would imply a beginning
B) Because it would make Him less powerful
C) Because He is immortal
D) Because it is a deficiency

Key Takeaway
Allah is eternally living; death is impossible because it would negate His pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will die. Does the fact that Allah is eternally living give you comfort or fear? How does your awareness of His eternal life influence your trust in Him today?
?
If death implies a beginning, and you have a beginning, what does that mean for your reliance on the One who has no beginning? Are you living as if you depend on the Eternal or on temporary things?
?
The passage proves that death cannot touch Allah. How should this truth change the way you face your own mortality? Will you let it drive you to seek closeness to the Living One, or will you ignore it?

Incapacity and Dislike: Perfect Power and Will
Sanusi enumerates the impossibilities of incapacity and dislike, with a refutation of causation and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why incapacity and dislike are impossible for Allah, and how they relate to His omnipotence and will.

Core Idea
Allah is all-powerful and perfectly wills all that occurs.

Sanusi continues with the impossibilities of incapacity (ʿajz) and dislike (karāhiyya). Incapacity is the opposite of power, and dislike is the absence of will. The passage also refutes the idea that beings come from Allah by causation or nature, which would imply the world's pre-eternity. This lesson unpacks these concepts and their theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on incapacity and dislike
Read the Arabic text and translation that define these attributes and the arguments against them.
The passage includes a lengthy refutation of causation and nature as sources of creation.
2
Understand incapacity and its implications
Incapacity (ʿajz) is the opposite of power (qudra). It would imply Allah cannot act, which contradicts His omnipotence.
Forms like languor (futūr) and weariness (naṣb) are included.
3
Analyze dislike and the refutation of causation/nature
Dislike (karāhiyya) is absence of will. The text argues that if beings come from Allah by causation or nature, the world would be pre-eternal, which is false. The diversity of the world proves it is not the effect of a single cause or nature.
The commentator distinguishes between agent by causation and agent by nature.

?

?

How does this diversity force you to recognize that every moment is a direct act of Allah's will? Are you living as if your circumstances are the result of random nature or as if every detail is willed by Him for a purpose? What specific thought pattern do you need to correct?

Muteness and Sensory Deficiencies: Negating Imperfect Communication
Sanusi enumerates the impossibility of muteness and related sensory deficiencies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why muteness and sensory deficiencies like deafness and blindness are impossible for Allah, and how this preserves His perfect attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is perfect and free from any defect or limitation.

The final set of impossible attributes includes muteness (bakam) and its senses: deafness and blindness. These are negated because they would imply a defect in Allah's ability to communicate or perceive, which contradicts His perfection. The passage also distinguishes between verbal silence and internal silence, affirming that only the latter is negated, as verbal speech is already impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on muteness and similar attributes
Read the Arabic text and translation that state the impossibility of muteness and related deficiencies.
The commentator notes that deafness and blindness are also included.
2
Understand the types of silence
The passage distinguishes between internal silence (sukūt nafsī) – lack of speech due to a defect – and verbal silence, which is not applicable to Allah since verbal speech is impossible for Him.
Internal silence is negated; verbal silence is not even considered.
3
Relate to Allah's attribute of speech
Negating muteness affirms that Allah speaks with a perfect speech that is not physical or defective.
This aligns with the necessary attribute of divine speech (kalām).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطرقُ العدم، ويسمى الفناء. - والمماثلة للحوادث؛ من جرميَّة أو عرضيَّة، أو حلول، أو اتصال أو انفصال، أو بعد أو قرب أو كبر أو صِغَر . - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى عدم القيام بنفسه؛ بأن يفتقر إلى محلّ أو مخصص . - وعدم الوحدانية، بأن يكون ذا كثرة في ذاته أو صفاته، أو يكون له شريك في فعل من الأفعال. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الجهل، مركباً أو بسيطاً، أو ما معناه : من ظنّ أو غفلة أو نسيان أو نوم، أو اشتغال بشأن. ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموتُ عن القدم، إذ نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو يصدق بالوجود بعد العدم الَّذي هو الحدوث بالعدم المنقطع بالوجود واللاحق . قوله : ( وطرقُ العدم): هو مساو لنقيض البقاء. قوله : (والمماثلة للحوادث هي مساوية لنقيض المخالفة. قوله : (عدم القيام بنفسه : هو نقيض القيام، وكذا عدم الوحدانية نقيض الوحدانية . قوله: (الجهل مركباً أو بسيطاً مقابلة العلم للأوَّل من مقابلة الضّدَّين، وللثّاني : من مقابلة العدم للملكة. قوله : (الموت) : مقابلته للحياة من تقابل العدم، والملكة إن قلنا : إنَّ المستحيلات والعجزُ، وما في معناه : من فتور أو نصب. - والكراهية : أي : عدم الإرادة بأن يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، أو تصدر الكائنات عنه تعالى بالتَّعليل أو بالطبع، لما يلزم من قِدَم العالم، الذي قام البرهان القاطع على حدوثه؛ وورد الشَّرع به؛ لأنَّه يجب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، والقائل بذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين، كما تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل بالعلة والفاعل بالطَّبع : من أنَّ العلة لا تتوقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع، والطَّبيعة تتوقف على ذلك. ومما يدلُّ على بطلانها اختلاف أنواع العالم على كثرتها، إذ معلولُ العِلَّة والطبيعة لا يختلف. - وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى البكم؛ أي: عدم الكلام بوجود آفة تمنع منه، وفي معناه السكوت النَّفسي . الموت عدم الحياة، وتقابل الضّدّين إن قلنا : إنَّه أمر وجودي. قوله : (والعجز ) : هو مساو لنقيض القدرة. قوله : (والكراهيَّة) هي مساوية لنقيض الإرادة. : قوله : (البكم ) : هو وما بعده من الصَّمم والعمى، إما من مقابلة الضّدّين، أو العدم والملكة . قوله : (الشكوت النَّفسي) : أي : وأمَّا السُّكوت اللفظيُّ؛ فلا يتوهم في حق الله ؛ لاستحالة الكلام اللفظي عليه تعالى .
And the ways of nonexistence, and it is called annihilation. And resemblance to contingent beings: whether corporeality or accidentality, or indwelling, or connection or separation, or distance or proximity, or bigness or smallness. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, to not be self-subsistent; such that He would need a place or a particularizer. And not being unique, such that He would have multiplicity in His essence or His attributes, or have a partner in any action. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, ignorance, whether compound or simple, or whatever implies it: like doubt or heedlessness or forgetfulness or sleep, or being occupied with some matter. And death is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, because it negates pre-eternity, for the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity, and it applies to existence after nonexistence which is origination by nonexistence cut off by existence and subsequent. His saying: 'And the ways of nonexistence': it is equivalent to the opposite of everlastingness. His saying: 'And resemblance to contingent beings' is equivalent to the opposite of dissimilarity. His saying: 'Not being self-subsistent': it is the opposite of self-subsistence, and likewise not being unique is the opposite of uniqueness. His saying: 'Ignorance, compound or simple': its opposition to knowledge for the first is a contradiction of opposites, and for the second is the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: 'Death': its opposition to life is the opposition of privation and possession if we say that death is nonexistence of life, and contradiction of opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity, and what is in its meaning: like languor or weariness. And dislike: that is, absence of will such that what He does not will occurs in His dominion, or that beings come from Him, may He be exalted, by causation or by nature, because it necessitates the pre-eternity of the world, against which decisive proof has been established for its temporal origination; and the Revealed Law came with it; because the cause must be concomitant with its effect and the natural property with its imprint, and the one who says that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims, as has preceded, and the difference between the agent by causation and the agent by nature has preceded: that the cause does not depend on the existence of a condition nor the absence of an impediment, while the nature depends on that. And among what indicates its falsity is the diversity of the types of the world despite their abundance, for the effect of the cause and the nature does not differ. And likewise it is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, muteness: that is, absence of speech due to a defect that prevents it, and in its meaning is internal silence. Death is non-existence of life, and it is the opposition of two opposites if we say that it is an existential matter. His saying: 'And incapacity': it is equivalent to the opposite of power. His saying: 'And dislike': it is equivalent to the opposite of will. His saying: 'Muteness': it and what follows of deafness and blindness are either from the opposition of opposites, or privation and possession. His saying: 'Internal silence': that is, as for verbal silence, it is not imagined of God, because verbal speech is impossible for Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the impossible attributes (al-mustahilat) of Allah, drawn from the Kharida text with commentary by al-Sawi. The author first enumerates the attributes that cannot be attributed to God, each contrasted with a necessary attribute.

The list begins with 'ways of nonexistence' (turūq al-ʿadam), also called annihilation (fanā'), which is the opposite of everlastingness (baqā'). Next is resemblance to contingent beings (al-mumāthala lil-ḥawādith), including corporeality, accidentality, indwelling, connection, separation, distance, proximity, bigness, smallness — these are all negated because God is unlike any created thing. Then, not being self-subsistent (ʿadam al-qiyām bi-nafsih) is impossible; this would mean needing a place or a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ). Also impossible is lack of oneness (ʿadam al-waḥdāniyya): having multiplicity in essence or attributes, or having a partner in any act.

Ignorance (jahl) is impossible, whether compound or simple, and anything implying it like doubt, heedlessness, forgetfulness, sleep, or occupation with affairs. Death (mawt) is impossible because it negates pre-eternity (qidam); the opposite of pre-eternity is non-pre-eternity (lā qidam), which would imply origination after nonexistence.

The commentary then explains each term: 'ways of nonexistence' equals opposite of everlastingness; 'resemblance' equals opposite of dissimilarity; 'not self-subsistent' equals opposite of self-subsistence; 'not unique' equals opposite of uniqueness. Ignorance is opposed to knowledge: for compound ignorance, it is contradiction of opposites (taqābul al-ḍiddayn); for simple ignorance, it is privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka). Death is opposed to life as privation and possession if death is non-existence of life, but as contradiction of opposites if death is considered an existential thing.

The next section discusses incapacity (ʿajz) and its meanings like languor or weariness, and dislike (karāhiyya) which is absence of will — that something occurs in His dominion against His will, or that beings originate from Him by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ). The author argues that such views imply the pre-eternity of the world, which is refuted by decisive proof and revelation. The agent by causation (al-fāʿil bil-ʿilla) and agent by nature (al-fāʿil bi-ṭ-ṭabʿ) are distinguished: the cause does not depend on conditions or absence of impediments, while nature does. The diversity of the world's types shows their falsity because effects of a single cause or nature do not differ.

Finally, muteness (bakam) is impossible: absence of speech due to a defect. This includes internal silence (sukūt nafsī). Verbal silence is not imagined of God because verbal speech is impossible for Him. The commentary adds that muteness, deafness, blindness are opposed either as contradictions of opposites or as privation and possession.

Key Terms
البكم
السكوت النفسي
السكوت اللفظي
الصمم
العمى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's silence means He does not speak.
Correction: God's speech is non-verbal and perfect; any form of muteness or defect is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'internal silence' (السكوت النفسي) refer to?
A) The absence of speech due to a defect
B) Not speaking verbally
C) Meditative silence
D) A form of ignorance

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is perfect and free from any defect; muteness, deafness, and blindness are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you ever consider that your words can be imperfect or even sinful? How does the perfection of Allah's speech challenge you to purify your own speech and intentions?
?
This lesson negates any deficiency from Allah's attributes. In what areas of your life are you tempted to think that Allah is not fully aware, hearing, or speaking to your situation? What would change if you truly believed His hearing and speech are flawless?
?
Allah's speech is free from silence due to defect. Are there moments when you remain silent out of fear or laziness when you should speak the truth? How can reflecting on Allah's perfect speech inspire you to use your voice for justice and mercy?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Impotence and Aversion: Attributes Impossible for Allah
Al-Sawi explains the impossibility of impotence and aversion, refuting causation/nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why impotence and aversion are impossible for Allah and how the refutation of causation/nature supports divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Impotence and aversion are privations, not positive attributes; they cannot belong to the Perfect.

Al-Sawi begins listing attributes that cannot possibly belong to Allah. He starts with impotence (al-‘ajz) and aversion (al-karāhiyah), explaining that these imply deficiency. He then refutes the idea that Allah acts by causation or nature, which would entail the eternity of the world—a notion contradictory to reason and revelation. This lesson unpacks these foundational points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on impotence and aversion
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully, noting the definitions and the argument against causation/nature.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ما في معناه' for synonyms.
2
Understand the refutation of causation and nature
Al-Sawi argues that if Allah were a cause or nature, the world would be eternal. Recognize why this is invalid based on proof and consensus.
The diversity of the world is used as evidence.
3
Grasp the key terms and their opposites
Identify that impotence is opposite of power, aversion opposite of will. These are not positive attributes but negations.
This aligns with the concept of attributes of negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستحيلات
والعجزُ، وما في معناه : من فتور أو نصب. - والكراهية : أي : عدم الإرادة بأن يقع في ملكه ما لا يريده، أو تصدر الكائنات عنه تعالى بالتَّعليل أو بالطبع، لما يلزم من قِدَم العالم، الذي قام البرهان القاطع على حدوثه؛ وورد الشَّرع به؛ لأنَّه يجب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، والقائل بذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين، كما تقدم، وتقدم الفرق بين الفاعل بالعلة والفاعل بالطَّبع : من أنَّ العلة لا تتوقف على وجود شرط ولا انتفاء مانع، والطَّبيعة تتوقف على ذلك.
ومما يدلُّ على بطلانها اختلاف أنواع العالم على كثرتها، إذ معلولُ العِلَّة والطبيعة لا يختلف.
- وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى البكم؛ أي: عدم الكلام بوجود آفة تمنع منه، وفي معناه السكوت النَّفسي .
الموت عدم الحياة، وتقابل الضّدّين إن قلنا : إنَّه أمر وجودي.
قوله : (والعجز ) : هو مساو لنقيض القدرة.
قوله : (والكراهيَّة) هي مساوية لنقيض الإرادة.

قوله : (الشكوت النَّفسي) : أي : وأمَّا السُّكوت اللفظيُّ؛ فلا يتوهم في حق الله ؛ لاستحالة الكلام اللفظي عليه تعالى .
- ويستحيل عليه تعالى الصَّمم ،والعمى تعالى الله عن ذلك علوّاً كبيراً .
The impossible attributes: impotence and what is in its meaning, such as weakness or fatigue. And aversion: meaning lack of will such that what He does not will occurs in His kingdom, or that beings proceed from Him by causation or by nature, due to the necessitation of the eternity of the world, which decisive proof has established its temporality, and the Sharee'ah has affirmed it; because the cause must be concomitant with its effect and nature with its natural object, and the one who says that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims, as has preceded. And the difference between the agent by causation and the agent by nature has preceded: that the cause does not depend on the existence of a condition nor the absence of a barrier, while nature depends on that. And among what indicates its invalidity is the diversity of the types of the world despite their multitude, for the effect of a cause and nature does not differ. Similarly, muteness is impossible for Him, Exalted, i.e., lack of speech due to an affliction preventing it, and in its meaning is psychological silence. Death is the absence of life, and it is the opposition of two opposites if we say it is an existential matter. His saying: (Impotence) is equivalent to the opposite of power. His saying: (Aversion) is equivalent to the opposite of will. His saying: (Muteness) and what follows it of deafness and blindness are either from the opposition of opposites or from privation and disposition. His saying: (Psychological silence) means: as for verbal silence, it is not imagined in the case of Allah due to the impossibility of verbal speech for Him, Exalted. And deafness and blindness are impossible for Him, Exalted—Allah is exalted above that with a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage enumerates and explains attributes that are impossible for Allah (al-mustahilat). It begins by listing impotence (al-‘ajz) and its synonyms like weakness and fatigue, which are negations of power. Then aversion (al-karāhiyah) is defined as lack of will, and the text refutes the idea that actions proceed from Allah by causation or nature, because that would entail the eternity of the world—a notion refuted by proof and consensus. The diversity of the world is cited as further evidence against causation/nature. Then muteness (al-bukm) is explained as absence of speech due to an impediment, with psychological silence as its equivalent. Death (al-mawt) is mentioned as the absence of life, and its status as an opposite is noted if considered existential. Glosses follow: ‘ajz equals opposite of power; karāhiyah equals opposite of will; bukm, deafness (ṣamām), and blindness (‘amā) are either opposites or privations; psychological silence excludes verbal silence which is impossible for Allah. Finally, deafness and blindness are explicitly stated as impossible for Allah. The passage systematically shows that any deficiency that implies dependence or change cannot be attributed to Allah.

العجز
الكراهية
العلة

D) The temporality of the world

Key Takeaway
Impotence and aversion are negations of perfection; attributing causation or nature to Allah is invalid as it would imply an eternal world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you ever catch yourself doubting that Allah has the power to remove it, or that He 'doesn't care'? How does knowing that impotence and aversion are impossible for Him change how you should speak to yourself in those moments?
?
You often attribute events to 'cause and effect'—a good grade because you studied, an illness because of germs. In light of this lesson, what would it take for you to see Allah's direct will behind every single occurrence, not as a distant cause but as the sole agent?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is free from any hint of weakness or dislike toward you, what would you do today that you currently avoid out of fear or despair? Name one specific action and commit to it after this lesson.

Muteness, Deafness, Blindness, and Death: More Impossible Attributes
Al-Sawi lists impossibilities of muteness, death, deafness, and blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why muteness, deafness, blindness, and death are considered impossible for Allah and how they relate to His perfection.

Core Idea
Speech without impediment and eternal life are essential to Allah—their opposites are impossible.

After establishing impotence and aversion, Al-Sawi continues listing attributes that cannot be attributed to Allah. He addresses muteness (lack of speech due to an affliction), explaining that psychological silence is its equivalent. He then mentions death as the absence of life, and finally deafness and blindness. This lesson clarifies that these are privations that contradict divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on muteness, death, deafness, blindness
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully, noting the definitions and glosses.
The passage ends with a declaration of Allah's transcendence.
2
Understand the distinction between verbal and psychological silence
Verbal silence is impossible for Allah because verbal speech is impossible; psychological silence is the absence of speech.
This reinforces that Allah speaks but without imperfection.
3
Grasp that death is the absence of life
Death is defined as absence of life, and its status as an opposite is mentioned if considered existential.
Life is a necessary attribute of Allah, so its absence is impossible.

When you remain silent about truth or justice, are you choosing wisdom or falling into a privation that only belongs to creation? How will you ensure your silence is not a form of muteness that Allah is free from?
?
You fear death, but do you realize that your fear stems from a privation that cannot touch your Creator? How does the Ever-Living nature of Allah transform your relationship with mortality?
?
You sometimes turn a blind eye to the needs of others—are you unconsciously adopting a quality that is only possible for created beings? What specific step will you take today to replace that blindness with awareness that honors the All-Seeing?

Refuting the Doctrine of Obligating God to Do What Is Best
Al-Sawi refutes the doctrine that God is obliged to do what is best for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine that God is obliged to do what is most beneficial for creation is rejected in Ash'ari theology, based on observable reality and the nature of divine power.

Core Idea
God's actions are not constrained by obligations; His mercy is free, and the world's suffering proves it.

This passage confronts a controversial theological claim: that God must always choose what is best for His creatures. By examining the logical consequences of that claim—and contrasting them with the world we actually see—the author shows why it must be false, preserving both God's absolute power and the reality of trial and suffering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument. Note that it starts with a strong condemnation.
Notice the repeated 'law' (if) constructions.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Find the statement that the author calls a heretical innovation. Understand what is being denied.
3
List the absurd consequences
The author gives several 'ifs' leading to contradictions. Count them and see how each contradicts observed reality.
There are six consequences.
4
Understand the definitions of ṣalāḥ and aṣlaḥ
The commentary clarifies these terms. See how they relate to faith, health, reward, and paradise.
Ṣalāḥ is opposite of corruption; aṣlaḥ is the better.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The argument preserves God's transcendence and the genuine nature of trials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى بدعة شنيعة وإساءة أدب ودخل في الجائز رعاية الصلاح والأصلح، إذ لو وجب عليه تعالى ما هو الأصلح في حقٌّ العبد ما وقعت محنة، وما خلق الله الكافر الفقير المعذَّب دنيا وأخرى، وما حصل ألم لطفل لا تكليف عليه، ولما كانت بعض البهائم والطُّيور في غاية الضعف والبلاء، ولَمّا كان لطلب الهداية وكَشْف الضُّرّ معنى؛ لوجوب إيصال ما هو الأصلح للعبد، ولما بقي في قدرة الله تعالى بالنسبة إلى مصالح العباد شيء آخر، إذ قد أتى على ما في وسعه من الأصلح الواجب . (ومن يقل فعل الصَّلاح وجبا - الألف للإطلاق - على الإله) قوله : رعاية الصَّلاح هو ما يقابل الفساد؛ كالإيمان في مقابلة الكفر، والصّحة في مقابلة المرض. وقوله : (والأصلح) : هو يقابل الصَّلاح ؛ كالثواب بلا تكليف في مقابلة التّواب مع التكليف، وكونه في أعلى الجنان في مقابلة كونه في الجنَّة قوله : (ما وقعت محنة ... إلخ) : أي : مع أنَّ المشاهد خلاف.
The statement about the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ and al-aṣlaḥ upon Him the Exalted is a heretical innovation and bad manners, and consideration of al-ṣalāḥ and al-aṣlaḥ falls within the permissible. For if it were obligatory upon Him the Exalted to do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for the servant, then no trial would occur, and God would not have created the disbeliever, the poor, the tormented in this world and the Hereafter, and no pain would have befallen a child without obligation, and some animals and birds would not be in extreme weakness and affliction, and seeking guidance and removal of harm would have no meaning, because conveying what is most beneficial for the servant would be obligatory, and nothing would remain in God's power with respect to the interests of the servants, since He would have already done everything within His capacity of the obligatory most beneficial. (And whoever says: Doing al-ṣalāḥ is obligatory upon God) He said: Consideration of al-ṣalāḥ is that which is opposite to corruption; like faith opposite disbelief, and health opposite disease. And His saying: (And al-aṣlaḥ) is that which is opposite to ṣalāḥ; like reward without obligation opposite reward with obligation, and being in the highest paradise opposite being in paradise. His saying: (no trial would occur... etc.) i.e., while what is observed is contrary.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that God is obliged (wājib) to do what is beneficial (al-ṣalāḥ) and most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creation. The author first declares this view a heretical innovation (bid'a shanī'a) and a breach of proper conduct adab. He then argues that consideration of what is beneficial and most beneficial falls under the category of what is permissible (jā'iz) for God, not obligatory. The proof is by reduction to absurdity: if God were obliged to do the best for every servant, then trial (miḥna) would not occur, God would not create the disbeliever or the poor sufferer, no child would feel pain (since they are not obligated), animals would not be weak, and seeking guidance or asking for relief would be meaningless because the best would already be guaranteed. Moreover, God's power would be exhausted, having already done all that is obligatory. The commentator then defines terms: al-ṣalāḥ is the opposite of corruption (fasād), illustrated by faith vs. disbelief, health vs. disease. Al-aṣlaḥ (the better) is contrasted with ṣalāḥ; e.g., reward without obligation vs. reward with obligation, and being in the highest paradise vs. paradise. He concludes that the observed reality (al-mushāhad) is the opposite of what the doctrine would entail, thus refuting it.

الأصلح
وجوب
البدعة
المحنة
التكليف
الهداية
كشف الضر
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's mercy obligates Him to always give the best to His servants.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this is not obligatory; it is within His discretion, and observable reality (suffering) disproves the claim of obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the doctrine of wujūb al-aṣlaḥ were true?
A) God would be limited in power and unable to create suffering.
B) All creatures would be in the highest paradise.
C) There would be no trials, no suffering of children, and no weak animals.
D) God would be unjust.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine that God must do what is best for creation is refuted by the existence of trials, suffering, and the limits it would place on divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you witness the suffering of an innocent child or a devout believer in poverty, does your heart ever murmur against God's goodness? After reading this lesson, how will you silence that murmur? What specific thought will you hold onto next time you are tested with doubt?

Think of a blessing you received recently—health, wealth, guidance. Did you receive it with a sense of entitlement, as if God owed it to you? This lesson declares that God is not obliged to give you the best. Will you now thank Him with deeper humility, knowing every good is a free gift? This passage says that if God were obliged to do the best, seeking guidance would be meaningless. Do you pray for guidance as if it's a right you demand or a mercy you beg for? When you make du'a for relief from hardship, do you truly feel that God is free to answer or not? How will your next supplication reflect the lesson that God is not bound by obligation?

Al-Sawi's Refutation of the Doctrine of al-Salah wa al-Aslah
Al-Sawi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is best for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is most beneficial for creation is both a theological innovation and contradicts observable reality.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by what benefits us; the world's trials prove it.

Al-Sawi confronts a central Mu'tazilite doctrine that imposes an obligation on God regarding creation's welfare. He argues this is not only disrespectful but also logically absurd, as it would eliminate the very purpose of trials and the diversity of existence. Let's read his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let Al-Sawi's words speak first.
Notice the poetic line embedded in the text.
2
Identify the claim and consequences
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim (that God must do what is most beneficial) and the series of 'if-then' consequences Al-Sawi lists. What would happen if this obligation were true?
Count the consequences: no trial, no disbelievers/poor, no child pain, no weak animals, meaningless prayer, God's power exhausted.
3
Understand key terms
Al-Sawi defines 'al-salah' (good) and 'al-aslah' (more beneficial). Grasp the distinction and how it fits into the argument.
'Al-aslah' is a higher degree than 'al-salah'.
4
Connect to observable reality
The passage ends by pointing to 'al-mushahad' (the observed). Reflect on how the world's suffering contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim.
This is the crux of the refutation: reality disproves the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى بدعة شنيعة وإساءة أدب ودخل في الجائز رعاية الصلاح والأصلح، إذ لو وجب عليه تعالى ما هو الأصلح في حق العبد ما وقعت محنة، وما خلق الله الكافر الفقير المعذَّب دنيا وأخرى، وما حصل ألم لطفل لا تكليف عليه، ولما كانت بعض البهائم والطيور في غاية الضعف والبلاء، ولما كان لطلب الهداية وكشف الضر معنى لوجوب إيصال ما هو الأصلح للعبد، ولما بقي في قدرة الله تعالى بالنسبة إلى مصالح العباد شيء آخر إذ قد أتى على ما في وسعه من الأصلح الواجب . (ومن يقل فعل الصلاح وجبا - الألف للإطلاق - على الإله) قوله : (رعاية الصلاح) هو ما يقابل الفساد؛ كالإيمان في مقابلة الكفر، والصحة في مقابلة المرض. وقوله : (والأصلح) : هو يقابل الصلاح؛ كالثواب بلا تكليف في مقابلة الثواب مع التكليف، وكونه في أعلى الجنان في مقابلة كونه في الجنة. قوله : (ما وقعت محنة ... إلخ) : أي : مع أنَّ المشاهد خلاف.
The claim of the obligation of what is good and what is more beneficial upon Him, exalted, is a heretical innovation and bad manners, and consideration of what is good and more beneficial enters into the permissible. For if it were obligatory upon Him, exalted, to do what is more beneficial for the servant, there would be no trial, and God would not have created the disbeliever, the poor, the one tormented in this world and the hereafter, and pain would not have befallen a child on whom there is no legal obligation, and some animals and birds would not have been in extreme weakness and affliction, and seeking guidance and the removal of harm would have no meaning, due to the obligation of conveying what is more beneficial to the servant, and there would remain no other thing in God's power with respect to the interests of creatures, since He would have already done what is within His capacity of the obligatory more beneficial. (And whoever says that doing good is obligatory - with the alif for release - upon God). His saying: (consideration of what is good) is that which is the opposite of corruption; like faith in contrast to disbelief, and health in contrast to sickness. And His saying: (and the more beneficial) is that which is in contrast to what is good; like reward without obligation in contrast to reward with obligation, and being in the highest gardens in contrast to being in paradise. His saying: (there would be no trial... etc.) means: despite the fact that what is observed is contrary.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that it is obligatory upon God to do what is more beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author begins by declaring this claim a heretical innovation (bid'a) and bad manners, affirming that considering what is good and best falls within the realm of what is permissible for God, not obligatory. He then provides a series of 'law' (if) clauses demonstrating the absurd consequences that would follow if such an obligation were true: there would be no trials in the world, God would not have created disbelievers or poor people suffering in both worlds, infants would not suffer pain, animals and birds would not be weak or afflicted, prayers for guidance and removal of harm would be meaningless, and God's power would be exhausted since He would have to provide every possible benefit. The author then quotes a line of poetry: 'And whoever says that doing good is obligatory upon God' (with a poetic alif). Then the commentary defines terms: al-ṣalāḥ is what is opposite of corruption, e.g., faith vs. disbelief; al-aṣlaḥ is a superior good compared to mere good, e.g., reward without obligation vs. reward with obligation, or being in the highest paradise vs. lower paradise. The final line notes that the observed reality contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الأصلح
الجائز
البدعة
محنة
التكليف
المشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believed that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for every creature at all times.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows this belief is an innovation and contradicts divine power, wisdom, and the observable reality of suffering and trials.

Checkpoint
What would be a logical consequence if God were obligated to do the most beneficial for each creature?
A) Trials and suffering would still exist.
B) God would not have created disbelievers or the poor.
C) Prayer would become more meaningful.
D) God's power would remain unlimited.

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to provide the most beneficial for His creatures; the existence of trial and suffering in the world refutes the Mu'tazilite claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says that if God were obligated to give the most beneficial, seeking guidance would have no meaning. When you make du'a, do you truly believe God is free to respond as He wills, or do you feel entitled to a certain answer? How can you transform your supplication into pure worship, not a demand?

This passage argues it is not unjust because God is not bound by that obligation. How does this truth change the way you react when you see innocent suffering? Does it drive you to humility or to complaint?

The Mu'tazila's Argument Against Vision of Allah and Its Refutation
Al-Sawi presents the Mu'tazila's strongest argument (shubhat al-muqabala) and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's strongest argument (shubhat al-muqabala) against the vision of Allah and how the Ash'ari response refutes it by redefining ru'ya as a special perception and rejecting analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Just as we know Allah without spatial constraints, we can see Him without them.

The Mu'tazila argued that seeing Allah would imply physical limitations, contradicting His transcendence. In this passage, Al-Sawi presents their strongest argument—the argument of facing (muqabala)—and refutes it by clarifying that ru'ya is a type of perception created by Allah, not bound by ordinary physical conditions. This refutation also challenges the method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the structure of argument and refutation emerge naturally.
Notice the logical flow from 'if seen' to a series of impossible consequences.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's chain of consequences
The argument lists five necessary consequences of vision: facing, direction/space, rays/distance, substance/accident, whole/part. Each is meant to show contradiction with Allah's nature.
The chain leads to the conclusion that vision is impossible.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The response redefines ru'ya as a special perception that Allah creates without physical prerequisites. It also rejects the analogy from worldly vision.
The example of knowledge shows that perception can exist without location.
4
Reflect on the disagreement
Consider why the Mu'tazila insisted on this argument and how the Ash'ari reply preserves both divine transcendence and the possibility of vision in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على رؤية الله تعالى وخالف في ذلك المعتزلة، فأحالوها متمسكين بشُبَه ؛ أقواها شبهة المقابلة، وتقريرها : أنّه تعالى لو كان يُرى لكان مقابلاً للرائي ضرورةً، فيكون في جهة وحَيّز، ويلزم اتصال الأشعة من الباصرة بالمرئي والمسافة بين الرائي والمرئي بحيث لا يكون بعيداً جداً، ولا قريباً جداً، ولكان المرئى إمّا جوهراً وإما عرضاً، ولكان المرئي إما كله فيلزم التناهي والحصر، وإما بعضه فيلزم التّبعيض والتَّجزؤ، واللوازم كلّها محالة فالملزوم مثلها . وحاصل الجواب ما أشرنا له سابقاً من أنَّ الرُّؤية عبارة عن نوع : من الإدراك يخلقه الله متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء، في أي محل شاء، فلا يلزم ما ذكر، وقياس الغائب على الشاهد فاسد، فكما أنّ العلم إدراك، وهم يعلمونه لا في مكان ولا جهة ولا محدوداً ولا محصوراً، فكذا الرّؤية نوع من أنواع الإدراك، فيدركونه كذلك، ومع انكشاف تام كما نصَّ عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في كثير من الأحاديث .
Discussion on the vision of Allah. And the Mu'tazila disagreed in that, so they deemed it impossible, clinging to specious arguments; the strongest of them is the argument of facing (muqabala), and its presentation: that if He were seen, He would necessarily be facing the seer, thus He would be in a direction and space, and the connection of rays from the eye to the seen and the distance between the seer and the seen would be necessary such that He is not very far nor very near, and the seen would be either a substance or an accident, and the seen would be either all of Him (necessitating finitude and limitation) or part of Him (necessitating division and partitioning). All the necessary consequences are impossible, so the same applies to that which entails them. And the summary of the answer is what we indicated previously: that vision (ru'ya) is a type of perception that Allah creates whenever He wills, for whatever He wills, in whatever locus He wills, so what was mentioned does not follow, and analogizing the unseen to the witnessed is corrupt. For just as knowledge is perception, and they know Him not in a place, nor a direction, nor limited, nor confined, so too vision is a type of perception, and they perceive Him thus, with complete unveiling as the Prophet (peace be upon him) stated in many hadiths.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the Mu'tazila's objection to the vision of Allah and its refutation. It begins by stating that the Mu'tazila disagree with Ahl al-Sunna on this issue, deeming vision impossible. They cling to specious arguments, the strongest of which is the argument of facing (muqabala). The argument is presented: if Allah were seen, He would have to be facing the seer, implying direction and space, requiring a connection of rays, proper distance, and the seen would be either a substance or accident, and either whole (necessitating finitude) or part (necessitating division). Since all these consequences are impossible for Allah, the premise of vision is also impossible. Then the answer is given: vision is a type of perception that Allah creates whenever and wherever He wills, so these physical constraints do not apply. The method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogizing the unseen to the witnessed) is invalid, as shown by the example of knowledge (ilm): we know Allah without spatial constraints, similarly vision can occur without such constraints. Finally, it is mentioned that the Prophet (peace be upon him) confirmed this in many hadiths.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
شبهة المقابلة (shubhat al-muqabala)
مقابلة (muqabala)
قياس الغائب على الشاهد (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid)
إدراك (idrak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah would necessarily imply physical limitations like facing, direction, and distance.
Correction: Ru'ya is a special perception created by Allah, not bound by physical conditions; it does not require those limitations, just as knowledge of Allah does not require location.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's strongest argument against the vision of Allah?
A) That vision requires distance and rays, which are impossible for Allah.
B) That vision is only possible in this world.
C) That the Prophet said it is impossible.
D) That Allah said 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument fails because ru'ya is a special perception created by Allah, not bound by physical conditions, just as knowledge of Allah is not bound by space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you unconsciously imagine Him in a place or form? How does this lesson's emphasis on ru'ya as a special perception challenge you to purify your conception of Allah from physical limitations?
?
The Mu'tazila relied on their own reasoning (qiyas) to deny the vision of Allah. In what areas of your faith do you find yourself trusting your own logic over clear texts? Will you submit to the unseen even when your mind resists?
?
If you truly believe that you will see Allah in the afterlife without spatial constraints, how should that hope transform your relationship with Him today? Are you living with that eager anticipation, or have you become comfortable with distance?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Proof from 'La Tudrikuhu'
Al-Sawi presents the Mu'tazila's textual proof and the Sunni response distinguishing idrak from ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars refute the Mu'tazila's claim that the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' disproves the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The verse denies comprehensive perception, not sight itself.

The Mu'tazila argue that the Qur'anic verse 'Vision does not perceive Him' proves Allah cannot be seen, even in the Hereafter. But Sunni scholars point out a crucial distinction: the Arabic word 'tudrikuhu' means 'comprehend' or 'encompass', not simply 'see'. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal and explains why it resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's Argument
Start by reading the first part of the passage, where Al-Sawi presents the Mu'tazila's claim: they say vision of Allah is impossible in both worlds, and some even consider it kufr. Their strongest proof is the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'.
Notice that they interpret 'tudrikuhu' as 'sees'.
2
Understand the Sunni Response
Now read the answer: 'la tudrikuhu' means 'does not comprehend', not 'does not see'. The verse is about comprehensive perception, not the act of seeing. Sights cannot fully encompass Allah, just as intellects cannot.
This preserves the possibility of seeing Allah without full comprehension.
3
Reflect on the Broader Implication
Consider why the Mu'tazila made this mistake: they clung to physical habits or philosophical inclinations. This shows how a subtle linguistic point can save correct theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك هو قوله : (فأحالوها) : أي : قالوا بعدم جواز رؤيته في الدُّنيا والآخرة، بل قال أكثرهم : تجويزها كفر . قوله : (متمسكين بشبه) : أي : عقليَّة ونقليَّة ذكر العقلية وترك النَّقليَّة، وأقواها قوله تعالى : (لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ) (١)، وهو وارد مورد المدح، فيكون إدراكه بالبصر نقصاً وهو عليه محال. وأجيب عن ذلك بأنَّ معنى لا تدركه لا تحيط به على أنَّه قال : لا تدركه، ولم يقل : لا تراه ; فالأبصار لا تحيط به، كما أنَّ العقول لا تحيط به. وبالجملة فالمعتزلة في مخالفتهم لأهل السُّنَّة قد مالوا عن الحقِّ، إما لتمسكهم بالعادات، وإمَّا لميلهم إلى القواعد الفلسفية، والله يهدي من يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم .
That is his saying: 'fa-ahaluha': i.e., they said there is no possibility of seeing Him in this world and the Hereafter; rather most of them said: deeming it possible is unbelief. His saying: 'mutamassikin bi-shubah': i.e., rational and transmitted [proofs]; he mentioned the rational and left the transmitted; their strongest is His saying (may He be exalted): 'Vision does not perceive Him' (Surah al-An'am: 103), and it is in the context of praise, so perceiving Him with sight would be a deficiency, which is impossible for Him. And it was answered that the meaning of 'la tudrikuhu' is 'does not comprehend Him', since He said 'la tudrikuhu' and didn't say 'la tarahu'; thus sights do not comprehend Him, just as intellects do not comprehend Him. In summary, the Mu'tazila in their opposition to Ahl al-Sunna have deviated from the truth, either due to their adherence to habits or due to their inclination towards philosophical principles. And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'fa-ahaluha' and 'mutamassikin bi-shubah' from the base text. It explains that the Mu'tazila said vision is impossible in both worlds, and some even consider it kufr. They have both rational and transmitted proofs; the strongest rational was already given, and the strongest transmitted is the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'. The Mu'tazila argue that this verse is in praise of Allah, implying that being seen would be a deficiency. The response is that 'la tudrikuhu' means 'does not encompass/comprehend' (la tuhitu bihi), as the verse says 'tudrikuhu' not 'tarahu', so it is about comprehension, not sight. Therefore, sights do not encompass Allah, just as intellects do not. The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila have deviated due to clinging to physical habits or philosophical inclinations.

Key Terms
تدركه (tudrikuhu)
إحاطة (ihata)
أبصار (absar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The verse actually denies comprehensive perception, not the act of seeing. The Arabic word 'tudrikuhu' means 'comprehend', not 'see'. Therefore, seeing Allah without full comprehension is possible.

Checkpoint
How did the answer refute the Mu'tazila's use of the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'?
A) By arguing that the verse refers to this world only.
B) By arguing that 'tudrikuhu' means 'comprehend' not 'see'.
C) By arguing that the verse is ambiguous.
D) By arguing that the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response to the Mu'tazila's proof from 'La tudrikuhu' hinges on the linguistic distinction between idrak (comprehension) and ru'ya (sight).
